E: SUMMARY PROGRAM ANALYSIS
(detailed assessments are in the program file, see Annex 8)
1. Economic and financial
[X] Cost-Benefit Analysis: NPV=NA; Social IRR: Grade 1: -7.8% Grade 7: 17.3%
[X] Cost-Effectiveness Analysis: 20% savings with a reduction by half in repetition and dropout rates.
[XI Sensitivity analysis: Private and social rates of return will double when repetition and dropout rates
are reduced by 50%.
[XI Benefit Incidence Analysis: Increased investments in primary education will have a pro-poor income
distributing effect.
The economic and financial analysis (in Annex 4) conducted for the ESSP consists of (i) demand analysis
and rationale for public intervention; (ii) cost-benefit analysis, including sensitivity analysis- (iii) fiscal
analysis; and (iv) analysis of cost-effectiveness measures. The analysis is summarized below.
(i) Rationale for public intervention
Universal basic education (complete primary in low income countries, lower secondary in middle-income
countries) is a principal precondition for sustained economic growth. It increases labor force productivity
in existing jobs; it enables the labor force to move more easily to new jobs associated with higher levels of
economic development; it is the prerequisite (for a segment of the population) for advancement to middle
level and higher level professions; it induces reduction in undesirably high fertility rates; it helps improve
the health status of the population; it builds up a strong national identity via the use of a common language
and common cultural values; and it increases social cohesion by demonstrating to disadvantaged population
strata that they too belong in the social mainstream.
Empirical research in countries covering the entire spectrum of social and economic conditions supports
these arguments. Retums to education investment arc strongly positive, and typically retums for basic
education are higher than for middle and higher education. Mozambique is no exception to this rule, as
shown in the next section (ii).
Given the critical importance of education, particularly basic education, to long-term economic growth, the
state has a strong interest in promoting the necessary investment in the sector. In practical terms, this
means that the state will have to secure almost the entire financing of necessary investments from local
public revenues and external aid flows to the public sector. Given widespread poverty, private education
can be expected to cover only limited numbers of the school age population and only among high-income
households (currently, private schools enroll about 2% of primary school students).
With reference to "necessary" investments, the foregoing argument suggcsts that they are the sum of
investments needed to (a) attain universal primary education, (b) secure sufficient secondary education
graduates for filling middle level positions and advancing to university studies, (c) secure sufficient
university graduates for filling high level positions, and (d) cmbark upon a massive literacy campaign
targeted to young people who never enter school or drop out of the education system too early and stay
illiterate.
Availability of public resources determines the pace at which education investment will take place.
Projected resource availability (section (iii) below), combined with the difficulty of forcing the rate of
progress beyond technically imposed limits, has determined that at the end of the five-year program



Project Appraisal Document
Mozambique Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP)                                   Page 26
(assuming that all planned activities to improve internal efficiency are realized), Mozambique's primary
education system (grades 1-7) will graduate about 25% of the corresponding school population, as opposed
to the desired 100%. Thus, the proposed program constitutes an early step in a long process of building up
Mozambique's human capital from a very low base: According to the 1996/97 national Household Survey
(IAF), only 17.2% of Mozambicans aged 18-65 had completed 5 years of education, while the
corresponding number for women was 10.4% and for the poor 13.9%; currently onlv 49.2% of children 7-
11 years old attend school, while the corresponding number for girls is 39% and for the poor 41 7%, and
lastly the main reasons of non-attendance in rural areas were the cost of education to households, children's
need to work, and the absence of a school in the village
(ii) Cost-benefit analysis
Rate of return analysis and sensitivity analysis. A simple rate-of-return calculation was used to estimate
the current returns to one additional year of schooling from grade 1 to 7. It is assumed that the market
wage increase, as a proxy for the marginal product of the accumulation of human capital, is nil after two
years of primary schooling. In other words, most children leaving the school system after two years will
not be able to acquire higher paid jobs or improve productivity. Costs to the parents are school materials
and fees as it is assumed that the opportunity costs from lost work is marginal for young children. Unit
costs formn the basis of the calculation of the social rate of return.
The results show a negative private and social rate of return after one year of schooling, a situation that
reflects currently high dropout rates and very low attaiment and the limited benefits accruing to the society
as a whole from individuals dropping out of schools after one year of education. But rates of return
increase rapidly after more than two years of schooling, reaching a value of 18.5% for households and
17.3% for society at large in grade 7.
Simple Rates of Return at Current Levels of Attainment (1997)
Level of Schooling   Monthly Wage/
Completed            Income         Private Return    Social Return
Illiterate          $17.4
Grade 1             $17.4             -1.5%            -7.8%
Grade 5             $27.8             15.6%            14.0%
Grade 7             $34.7             18.5%            17.3%
The sensitivity analysis reveals a clear need to improve internal efficiency throughout the system. By
reducing repetition and dropout rates by 50%, a target set under the new program, both the social and
private IRR will double . It should be noted that the computed rates of return do not attempt to capture
the higher productivity of graduates expectedfrom the better quality of education resultingfrom the
envisaged improvements. Better quality of graduates would tend to raise IRR over the estimated value.
These results are reinforced by computations donc by Mozambican researchers based on the 1996-97
Household Survey. Thus, a female completing Grade 7 increases household per capita consumption by
36% in rural areas and 25% in urban areas. Also, children of literate mothers are significantly better
nourished, are more likely to complete necessary vaccinations, and more likely to be issued health cards.
Impact on poverty. The benefit incidence analysis revealed a pattern common among developing countries.
Under the ESSP program, marginalized groups, including girls and the poorest, will particularly benefit
from the ESSP as targeted increased investments in primary education will have a pro-poor income
distributing effect. This will be reinforced by the planned increase in education expenditure per student.



Project Appraisal Document
Mozambique Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP)                                 Page 27
(iii) Fiscal analysis
Fiscal impact. The total program for the five-year period 1999-2003 is costed at US$717'million. This
amount covers capital as well as recurrent expenditure for primary education (grades 1-7), secondary
education (grades 8-12) and technical education, but excludes expenditures on higher education and the
effects of planned public sector salary decompression on education costs. An additional expenditure of
US$101 million and US$100 million (for the period 1999-2003) is estimated for salary decompression and
for higher education respectively. Thus, the total projected cost (capital and recurrent) for the provision of
education services at all levels could amount to about US$91 8 million in 1999-2003, or US$818 million
without higher education.
The fiscal viability of education sector expansion plans can be checked by comparing the above figures to
macroeconomic projections worked out by Bank and IMF staff in collaboration with Mozambican
authorities. The Government will have to allocate up to 16% of projected public expenditures (US$918
million over US$5,767 million) to meet the public sector financed costs of this program (including higher
education) over the 1999-2003 period. The Minister of Finance, in his Letter of Sector Policy to the Bank,
commits the Govemment to allocate up to 21 % of public expenditures to the education sector by the year
2003. The fiscal viability of the proposed program of education sector expansion is ensured both in termns
of the country's economic prospects and the Government's commitment to the sector's development. Also,
education (including universities) would absorb an average of 4.4% of GDP per annum in the period 1999-
2003, which is modest in view of the high percentage of school-age children and the need to rebuild
infrastructure badly damaged during the war.
Impact on unit costs. Education expenditures in Mozambique, measured either as a share in GDP or in
total public expenditures, remain below the Sub-Saharan Africa average. Under the new program unit
costs will increase as a result of the increase in salaries, the increase in spending on school materials (a
one-to-one pupil-textbook ratio) and the increase in spending on pedagogical training. Per student costs of
primary education are estimated to increase by 40% from US$14 to about US$ 19. While the unit cost will
increase considerably at primary level, it will only change slightly for lower secondary education (from
US$65 to about $70 per pupil) as the program will increase the student-teacher ratio from about 22 to 28,
thus partially offsetting increases in teacher salaries. Primary teacher salaries (EP I: US$37 per month;
EP2: US$78) are below the Sub-Saharan average of about US$70 for primary education, but will also
increase under the proposed program as a direct result of the salary decompression and upgrading program.
Sub-sectoral allocation of resources. Under the program, allocations to the sub-sectors will be improved
following the trend since 1994. Before 1994 primary education received less than 40% of the total
education budget, while at the same time the administrative overhead of the system was very high at 21 %.
By the year 2001, without an introduction of new programs for technical and vocational training and higher
education, administration will absorb 12% of total public expenditures on education, primary education
49%, secondary education 14%, teacher training 6%, technical and vocational training 4%, tertiary
education 12% and others 3%.



Project Appraisal Document
Mozambique Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP)                                   Page 28
(iv) Cost-effectiveness analysis
An attempt has been made to compute the cost savings from improvements introduced through the
proposed program (mainly, a reduction in the dropout rates and repetition rates, which reach up to 50%).
The method adopted and the full results of these computations are presented in Annex 4. In sumimary,
because of the lack of some necessary data, two approximations were made. First, numbers of graduates
over a number of years at the various levels of primarv and secondary education were related to education
expenditures in those same years instead of, as they should be, to expenditures in the vears that these
graduates were going through the education system. Second, per unit costs of graduates at the various
levels were approximated by relative teacher costs for these levels The computations based on these
approximations indicate that the cost savings from policv reforms and other interventions promoting
internal efficiency amount to about 20%. In other words, the required expenditure for producing the
specified numbers of graduates at the various levels of primary and secondary education is expected to be
20% less than it would be if dropout rates and repetition rates remained unchanged at their 1996 levels,
even though cost per student is expected to rise as mentioned in section (iii) above.
2. Technical
School construction. The Govemment has stressed the need to place the school at the center of the
education system. Traditionally, school places have been expanded in two wa's. Communities build
schools at very low cost using locally available materials and methods, without any Government financial
or technical support. In addition, the Government normally built schools of cement blocks through
contractors, often at very high cost. To expand the school system as much as possible within the budget
envelope available, the program will gradually introduce a Community School Program (CSP) that puts the
community at the center of school construction and maintenance. The design of primary school classrooms
will be based on cost-effective models used in Mozambique and in the region, which are suitable for
construction by communities as well as contractors (see Annex 13 for details). About 5,000 classrooms
will be built through the CSP over five years.
Demand and supply for teachers. MINED recognizes that the current number of pre-service teacher
training graduates will be inadequate to cover the proposed expansion and improvements to the education
system and therefore relies on an expanded in-service teacher training program. Overall about 8,500
teachers without pre-service training will have to be hired from the 10th grade and trained through this in-
service training program. At the moment the expansion in the number of teachers and the hiring of 1 0th
grade graduates is viable from both a fiscal and supply side. The pressure on the system will be further
eased if the proposed improvements in the internal efficiency are implemented for both basic education and
the teacher training system. In the ESSP scenario (Table 7 (b) in Annex 4), the population census for 1997,
which estimates overall population at about 20% below the estimations based on the 1980 census, has been
used. Under this scenario the proposed expansion of the education system becomes more feasible, as the
need for teachers will go down by about 30%, easing the pressure of hiring 10th grade graduates and
making the upgrading of untrained teachers viable within 5-6 years. This will reduce the overall costs of the
program in the later years, as the initial investments for the first years will remain the same. The analysis of
teacher training is described in detail in Annex 16.
Expansion of upper primary education. Upper primary schools (grades 6 and 7) are usually separate
schools where teaching is done by subject specialist teachers. It is normally difficult to establish these
schools because of a lack of adequately qualified teachers and the need for a minimum number of students



Project Appraisal Document
Mozambique Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP)                                   Page 29
to make the school viable. Expanding the second cycle of primary education as currently designed was
rejected because of the potentially high cost of opening schools and hiring teachers who may not be fully
utilized. The decision was therefore to unify the two cycles by adding grades 6 and 7 to lower primary
schools. This system will make it easier for grade 5 graduates to continue to grade 6. It will also make
better use of specialist teachers, who may be underutilized in separate upper primary schools.
Teacher training and support. The Government proposed to replace the three-year teacher training
program for 7th grade graduates (7+3) with a two-year program for I 0th grade graduates ( 10+2).
However, because of the shortage of 10th grade graduates to enroll in teacher training programs, the
Government has decided to continue the 7+3 programs in the short term. At the same time, it will expand
places for the 10+2 program and gradually introduce a new, accelerated training program (called 10-+ 1 + 1).
The second year of this new program will be spent as full-time supervised teaching. In addition, 15.000
teachers with fewer than three years of training after the 7th grade will be upgraded through the in-service
training program provided by the Instituto de Aperfeicoamento de Professor (IAP - the Teacher In-service
Training Institute). The IAP program is one successful example of distance education reaching large
numbers of highly dispersed learners at a relatively low cost. The program would also emphasize dynamic
teacher support and school operations by rebuilding the ZIP system. These ZIPs would link schools with
pre- and in-service teacher training, allow for regular teacher seminars, provide pedagogical support to
schools, and help develop school-community relations. (See Annex 16 for more information about teacher
training).
Learning materials. At the moment, the Government-owned textbooks procurement unit, Diname, is the
country's only publisher of textbooks. The planned transition from state monopoly to competition in
publishing will be phased in roughly by the end of the program period. There is concern that opening the
market for textbook publishing will initially reduce the availability of textbooks and other learning
materials in the schools. Publishing skills are limited, and the market for printed materials in Mozambique
is relatively small. The issue of textbook availability is complicated by the proposed policy of cost-
recovery for students in grades I to 5, which could seriously limit book sales (because poverty is
widespread, the Government will continue to provide free books to students up to grade 5 and to
disadvantaged students through the Caixa Escolar). In the higher grades, the Government will introduce
progressive cost-recovery for textbooks. The Government will follow a carefully planned program of
transition to privatization following the steps outlined in the report on textbooks publication, to ensure
continued provision of books in schools while the private sector develops its capacity. These transition
arrangements will also include a buffer fund for the purchase of books during the transition.
Cycles of learning. To improve the internal efficiency and unclog the system at the primary level, the
option of introducing automatic promotion was considered. This was rejected because a low-quality
system with poorly qualified teachers, many with limited commitment, would provide no checks on student
achievement. However, to reduce the burden of excessive examination, a new system will be introduced to
assess student achievement at specific points. This change will organize the school system according to
cycles of learning instead of grades to permit a better timeframe for curriculum development and learning
outcomes.
3. Institutional
Capacity building. Mozambique's administration system has traditionally been centralized, and the
implementation of the strategic plan under a policy of decentralization will impose new challenges.
Decentralization in the management of education will be achieved by establishing an appropriate



Project Appraisal Document
Mozambique Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP)                                       Page 30
administrative framework for the central, provincial, district and school levels, including regulations
relating to interaction among them. The qualifications and grade levels of staff will be upgraded to meet
the new responsibilities. To address these issues, the program will develop and gradually implement a
capacity building strategy. Within the Government. the program will increasingly incorporatc district
proposals into the planning process. Annex 12 contains a discussion on issuies, progress so.far and future
plans for institutional development
4. Social
Gender. The program will help implement MINED's plan of action for incorporating gender initiatives in
the ESSP (prepared February 1998). The 1996/97 national Household Survey (IAF) found that education
for women in rural areas was associated not only with higher household consumption but also with social
benefits related to the health, nutritional status and schooling of their children But socioeconomic,
geographical and cultural constraints cause great variance in girls' enrollment and completion rates across
regions. Girls' share of total enrollment in lower primary, upper primarv and secondarx education is,
respectively, only 30%, 27% and 21% in the North, and 36%. 29% and 31% in the Center Dropout ratcs
are similar for boys and girls at each grade level, but repetition rates are significantly higher for girls in
every grade and every region. MINED's report recommends incorporating the gender dimension into
policy and planning, developing MINED capacity to "mainstream" gender issues in the ESSP, cnsuring
equitable access to recruitment and promotion, and increasing the number of female students and teachers
Gender Units will monitor such initiatives as providing scholarships for girls, providing safe boarding
facilities for female students and incorporating gendcr concerns into the curriculum and teacher training
Vulnerable groups. The program targets activities to rural areas and to underserved provinces where the
incidence of poverty is substantially above average Activities across the program's components
specifically emphasize the necds of poorer regions and households. Most of the construction and
rehabilitation program will concentratc on rural areas The Government will address the shortage of
qualified teachers in rural areas through qualifications upgrading, incentives and improved facilities. The
Caixa Escolar will ensure that the poorest sections of society rceive educational matcrials, and the revised
curriculum will better reflect rural needs and realities. The shift in management responsibility and
resourccs, along with accompanying skills development, will give rural communities greater control.
5. Environmental assessment
Environmental Category:       I I A    I 1 B    [XI C
There will bc no environmental problems arising from the program. The program will build schools in
rural areas. Water, soil, sand and othcr local materials will be used for construction. Sanitation facilities
will be built at a number of schools, and these will bc sited to avoid contaminating water supplies. The
program will provide water to schools where possiblc to improve health conditions. The program will also
promote environmcntal awareness through the curriculum and education materials.
6. Participatory approach
Primary beneficiaries and other affected groups. The Govemnment's Strategic Plan was developed with
the participation of a full range of stakeholders including parents, local communities employers, NGOs,
and religious organizations. This was achieved through: (a) bringing teachers and staff into the decision-
making process, through the various working groups, (b) increasing representation of actors at the



Project Appraisal Document
Mozambique Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP)                                   Page 31
provincial, district, and school levels in the planning process, (c) strengthening ZIPs and training school
directors to interact more effectively with communities; and (4) holding consultative sessions with civil
society. Specific consultative meetings included:
(i) April 1998 - a seminar opened by the Prime Minister on the main objectives and strategies of the
Education Sector Strategic Plan, especially on technical education and vocational training, with
participants from Government ministries, private sector, higher education institutions, trade unions, and
NGOs.
(ii) May 1998 - The Prime Minister and the President addressed a seminar on the ESSP and strategies for
translating its objectives into practice, for central, provincial and district level directors, and key staff.
(iii) May 1998 - a seminar attended by representatives from national and intemational NGOs, churches,
trade unions and educators on the ESSP and strategies for community participation in the ESSP
implementation.
(iv) August 1998 a seminar for about 40 top officials from the 11 provinces and the HQ in Matola, on
organizational change and leadership for the ESSP implementation.
Similar workshops are planned for the future.
F. SUSTAINABILITY AND RISKS
1. Sustainability
The proposed program and expansion ($818 million) including salary decomposition of US$ 101 million
will require public expenditures for education, excluding higher education, to rise by about 10% annually,
mainly to cover the increased salary costs of the greater number of teachers and the salary decompression
of about 29%. As a share in total recurrent expenditures the share will increase from 15% in 1998 to
18.7% by year 2003. Measured against GDP the share will remain constant at 4.4% including higher
education. These ratios are consistent with the targets set out in the HIPC Decision Paper. In view of
projected increases in public resources, and the Government's pledge to gradually raise education's share
of public expenditures up to 21% by year 2003, the program is fiscally feasible.
Although the projected flows of foreign aid imply that the program will continue to rely on donors'
assistance, long-term sustainability of the program seem ensured cven if donor assistance were to stabilize
at current levels. The country's prospects for cconomic growth are good and the Government is committed
to increasing the share of education in the budget.

G. MAIN CREDIT CONDITIONS
1. Conditions of effectiveness
(a)    Submission of the Program Implementation Manual, including detailed designs of all components
as well as schedule and costs for the first two years of implementation.
(b).   Completion of the Financial Procedures Manual.
(c)    Completion of the procurement plan for the first two years.
(d)    Appointment of the Financial Controller and the Deputy Financial Controller.
2. Other conditions
(a)    Submission of a plan for the involvement of communities in the establishment and running of
schools by December 31, 1999.
(b)    Completion of a census of ZIPs and a plan for expansion of their activities by December 31, 1999.
(c)    Joint annual Program implementation reviews in May aimed at: (i) documenting progress toward
objectives; (ii) identifying and resolving obstacles to Program implementation; and (iii) adjusting targets
and corresponding programs to reflect progress achieved in the implementation of the program in prior
years and ensuring responsiveness to changes to effectively achieve the objectives of the program.
(d)    Submission annually by October 1, of an annual work program and budget (AWPB) consistent
with the program which will form the basis of the annual review.
(e)    Submission by the end of the first and third quarters of each year, of a progress report including
information on agreed outcome and performance indicators based on data from the Program Monitoring
Reporting system. The report would include, inter alia, a summary statement on the status of
implementation of each component, including compliance with project covenants and progress in meeting
agreed policy objectives.
(f)    Submission by May 31, 2001, of a mid-term report integrating the results of the monitoring and
evaluation activities of the program on the progress achieved during the period from program effectiveness,
and a work program covering the period until project completion.
(g)    Submission by June 30, 2000, of a policy strategy and work program for privatization of textbook
publication.
(h)    Submission by December 31, 1999, of an action plan for the improvement of internal efficiency
(dropout and repetition).
(i)    Submission by December 31, 1999, of a specific plan and procedures for the management of direct
grants to schools.

MOZAMBIQUE
Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP)
Detailed Program Description
The objective of the Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP) is to provide increased and equitable
access to higher quality education and to improve the management of education for economic and social
development in Mozambique. IDA will fund key elements of the ESSP related mainly to primary and
secondary education, as well as helping to develop strategies for other subsectors. The Government is yet
to finalize a comprehensive framework for the development of technical and vocational education, adult
literacy, and higher education. Once interventions for basic education are underway, donors will discuss
specific plans for the development and integration of the other subsectors into the program.
Component 1: Quality of Education, US$ 108.5 million (IDA: US$ 25.8 million)
Objective. The objective of this component is to improve the quality and internal efficiency of primary and
general secondary education by reducing dropout and repetition rates and improving student academic
achievements, especially in rural and underserved areas, and among girls.
1.1    Teacher training (US$ 24.7 million total; IDA US$ 6.0 million). The ESSP assigns the highest
priority to pre-service and in-service teacher training. About 7,600 teachers (or about a quarter) are un-
trained at the lower primary level and the corresponding ratio at upper primary is about 21%. In addition
to upgrading existing teachers, the Government will also need to hire new teachers to meet its enrollment
targets. An estimated 18,700 new teachers will be needed during the program period. With new planned
capacity and an increase in efficiency, teacher training institutes will be able to graduate an estimated 8,400
new teachers over this period. The gap will be filled with grade 10 graduates, and complemented through
intensified in-service training. (See Annex 16 for more information about the teacher training system and
tcacher demand and supply).
At the pre-service level, three types of institutions will continue to operate in the short termn-i) Curso de
Formacao de Professores Primarios (CFFP), providing three years of training for lower primary candidates
with seven years of schooling; (ii) Institutos Medios de Professores Primarios (IMPs), providing three years
of training for upper primary teachers (10+3); and (iii) Institutos de Magisterio Primario (IMAPs),
providing two years of training for all primary teachers (10+2). The Government, however, will move
toward a 10+1 + I model that will consist of 10 years of schooling as entrance qualification, I year of
intensive pre-service training, and I year of supervised practice accompanied by in-service training and
support. Because of a shortage of grade 10 graduates, the Government has decided to continue its 7+3
program in the short term. At the same time, it will expand places for the 10+2 program and gradually
introduce 10+1 + 1. Under the plan to unify the two levels of primary school, fewer teachers will be needed
for each upper primary grades. so the IMPs will gradually be phased out as the IMAP system expands
enrollment. The program will establish five new IMAPs and upgrade four CFPPs into IMAPs.
At the in-service level, the program will expand training and instructional support for teachers through the
current programs of the Instituto de Aperfeicoamento Pedagogico (Teacher Improvement Institute, or IAP)
by distance learning and the ZIPs. IAP will upgrade 15,000 un-trained and under-qualified teachers-
Group E teachers (graduates with seven years of schooling)-up to the D level. This will provide these
teachers with a diploma equivalent to the one provided by the primary teacher training course CFPP. IAP
will also improve the qualifications of 6,000 D-level teachers with no professional training (10 years of
schooling). Overall, about 25% of primary teachers and 50% of secondary teachers will receive in-service



Annex 2
Page 2 of 8
training. School directors and supervisors will also participate in training programs for school quality
improvements. The program also intends to increase the motivation of teaching staff by improving the
working conditions in the classrooms, and by establishing training and resource centers (Nucleos de
Formacao Permanentes, or NUFORPEs) for in-service training in the provinces. The Government will also
revise teachers' career development procedures to limit delays in teacher promotion following
qualifications.
The program will finance (a) design, construction, and furmishing of five IMAPs and one IAP; (b)
workshops and seminars for teacher trainers, (c) leaming materials. (d) the costs of pedagogical monitoring
and supervision; (e) rehabilitation of continuing education centers (NUFORPEs); and (f) in-service and
distance education costs including course design, training, and materials.
1.2    Pedagogical support for teachers (US$ 17.7 million total; IDA US$ 6.2 million). MINED will
also improve quality by reinforcing the ZIPs (Zonas de Influencia Pedagogica-pedagogical zones of
influence), clusters comprising about 6 to 10 schools each. Although the ZIP program is about 40 years
old, it has declined to become mainlv a forum for teachers to discuss problems. The program will revitalize
the ZIPs to provide in-service training and pedagogical support to tcachers-building the skills and
confidence of new or untrained teachers and keeping experienced teachers up-to-date and engaged. The
ZIPs are being associated with teacher resource centers to be based in each district. The ZIPs link schools
with pre- and in-service teacher training, allow for regular teacher seminars, provide pedagogical support to
schools, and help develop school-community relations The program will rehabilitate 840 ZIPs (ZIP census
to be undertaken in 1999), which will serve as models for the others. MINED will strengthen pedagogical
supervision and school inspection services, allowing teaching activities to be systematically monitored and
supported. While a shortage of teachers persists, about a third of primary schools will continue with double
and triple shifts. To smooth out this problem, the ministry will ensure that all training programs for
tcachers provide them with skills and techniques that will enable them to work with large classes.
The program will finance (a) rehabilitation of 840 ZIPs, (b) teaching materials for ZIPs; (c) a national
seminar on ZIPs-related experience, (d) study tours related to ZIP methodologies; (e) pedagogical technical
assistance; (f) training for ZIP coordinators; and (g) operational expenses for ZIP models.
1.3    Curriculum transformation (US$ 7.1 million total; IDA US$ 1.0 million). The program
supports transformation of the curriculum to make it more relevant to the rapidly changing requirements of
Mozambican society, and particularly to address the sociocultural and economic needs of rural areas and
gender issues. The Instituto Nacional de Desenvolvimento da Educacao (INDE) is leading the curriculum
revision effort, with UNESCO support. The revision involves teachers and representatives of civil society
such as NGOs, workers' and employers' organizations, and religious organizations.
The existing curriculum does not clearly specify the content to be taught at each grade and needs to be more
consistent with learning goals and the assessment system. The Government aims to ensure a systematic
progression of pupils through the primary grades. "Cycles of learning" strategies will allow for longer
periods for children to absorb material. Revising the curriculum is also necessary to reflect integration of
lower primary and upper primary into one comprehensive basic education subsystem. It must be
sufficiently rigorous to provide young Mozambicans with the academic skills that they need and sufficiently
flexible to accommodate the diverse demands and expectations of a pluralistic society.



Annex 2
Page 3 of 8
The curriculum revision is also promoting "education for peace, democracy and national reconciliation"
pilot initiatives and "systematic action in the field of moral education and civic awareness in order to
combat delinquency and corruption" (Council of Ministers Resolution no. 8/95)-an important aim for a
post-conflict country like Mozambique. It will also incorporate any adopted strategies related to language
of instruction in the initial grades of lower primary, including the use of mother tongue languages for
instruction in grades 1 and 2. The program will adapt materials to support girls' learning needs and also
pilot an environment and health education program.
The program will finance (a) international and local technical assistance in curriculum revision and
technical evaluation for various subjects including math, science and bilingual education; (b) equipment and
fumiture; (c) training and fellowships related to curriculum revision; (d) operational expenses including
printing and distribution, and (e) seminars on democracy and national reconciliation.
1.4    Learning materials (US$ 39.9 million total; IDA US$ 6.4 million). The program goal is one
book for every pupil per subject in primary school. The cost of school materials will be shared by the state
and parents, local communities and other agencies (e.g., NGOs, religious organizations, and the private
sector), but textbooks will be free to pupils in primary grades 1 to 7. The Government established the
national social program Caixa Escolar, to support education at the community level (Ministerial Decree
No. 47/89 of December 28, 1989). This system is funded by the Government and donors and will ensure
equity in access to school materials among the poorest sections of society. It will provide kits of basic
educational materials for teachers and classrooms. The program will give ZIPs the ability to directly
purchase and/or select specific titles. ZIPs will be able to introduce libraries and alternative/multiple
textbooks for use in school.
Although the supply of books in classrooms has increased in recent years, the Government wants to ensure
long-tern supply. For that reason, the program will gradually implement a new textbooks policy to
encourage the development of commercial publishing. With technical assistance,* MINED has begun
mapping its strategy for encouraging the development of an open market in publishing to improve the
efficiency. capacity and sustainability of textbooks production and distribution. It has also established the
Working Group for Basic Leaming Materials to coordinate the process and recommend policy steps. The
program will fund further activities to complete this shift. The steps for moving to privatization are
outlined in the consultants report of March 1998 on which MINED's policy is based. These steps were
reviewed at a seminar in August 1998.
At the moment, the Government-owned publishing house, Diname, is the country's only procurement
agency for textbooks. INDE, as the writer, holds the copyright for primary school textbooks, while Diname
produces school textbooks. To ensure quality, INDE will continue to set standards, evaluate books, and
provide training, but commercial publishers will be encouraged to compete with Diname for the right to
produce textbooks. All books produced, at all levels of the system, will be submitted for the approval of the
Conselho de Aprovacao do Livro. Foreign publishers will be allowed to participate if they establish
themselves, and publish, in Mozambique. They must also build local knowledge through training and the
transfer of technology.
The transition from state monopoly to competition in publishing will be gradual, to be phased in by the end
of the program period. To ensure that textbooks will continue to be produced and delivered to schools
during the transition, the program will also institute a buffer fund. The fund will correspond to
Graphium Consult AB, "Towards a National Policy for Educational Materials in Mozambique," March 12, 1998.



Annex 2
Page 4 of 8
approximately one year's supply of textbooks to the entire general education system. The Government (in
1994) has already taken one important step toward creating an open market in textbooks provisions-the
decentralization of textbooks procurement to the provincial or district level. Private traders already
distribute books to schools.
The intention is that the distribution of IDA funds will be modeled on the Caixa Escolar system, as outlined
in the Plano Operativo da Caixa Escolar. The system is already used for the procurement and distribution
of learning materials totaling US$6.5 million annuallv, and funds are disbursed within a few months of
receipt. Under this system, annual budgets and distribution plans are prepared at the district level in
collaboration with schools and communities and approved by MINED and the Ministry of Planning and
Finance (MPF). Separate bank accounts (administered by a provincial treasurer) are established for each
province, and sub-accounts are established for districts with local capacity.
The program will finance (a) about 43 million primarv school textbooks, 375,000 teacher manuals, and
other teaching/learning materials, (b) technical assistance for the commercialization of materials
production: (c) technical assistance to monitor textbook distribution; (d) materials, technical assistance, and
training to expand bilingual education, and (e) support for evaluation and supervision of bilingual
education, including equipment and furniture.
1.5    Assessment and examinations (US$ 2.4 million total; IDA US$ 0.3 million). To reduce the
burden of excessive examination, the ESSP will introduce a new system to assess student achievement at
specific points, in line with the revision of the curriculum and the introduction of complete schools (grade I
to 7). This approach will be organized according to "cycles of learning" instead of grades to pennit a better
timeframe for curriculum development and learning outcomes. The program will develop and implement a
system for the collection of data on student achievement in primary schools, including a review of the
present examination system. Systematic monitoring and assessment will enable the Ministry to track the
performance of the education system and identify aspects of the curriculum or teaching methodology that
need improvement. In addition, the ministry will continue to work with educators to modify instructional
and assessment practices that contribute to high rates of failure and repetition. MINED will also encourage
school directors and teachers to develop procedures for periodically conveying information about students'
performance to their parents or guardians.
The program will finance (a) national and international technical assistance in the concepts and logistics of
conducting examinations; (b) examinations training; (c) study tours for supervisors; and (d) related
equipment, equipment and operating costs.
1.6    Direct support for schools (US$ 13.6 million total; IDA US$ 5.0 million). The program will
create a School Quality Improvement Fund to support the decentralization of resource administration to the
school level. The fund will be allocated to individual schools in the form of annual grants, which will be
spent on the most pressing education needs. The grant will be managed by the school council, composed of
teachers, parents' representatives, the school director and the district director. Initially, an NGO operating
under the general authority of the district director will support the procurement and distribution of goods to
meet identified school needs. (The institutional development component funds a parallel program to
strengthen district accounting and financial management.) Pilot activities in school feeding and school
health may also be introduced to improve the learning environment.
The program will finance the cost of grants to schools.



Annex 2
Page 5 of 8
1.7    Training of school directors (US$ 3.1 million total; IDA US$ 0.9 million). MINED's
decentralization efforts imply the need to strengthen capacity at every level. International research has
shown that leadership is an important element in school effectiveness. ESSP therefore supports a broad
training effort to strengthen the leadership skills of more than 6,000 school directors. The training will aim
to build competence in school management, the supervision of teachers, school finance, community-school
relations, best academic practices, and the collection and use of data.
The program will finance materials, travel and other training expenses for 6,262 directors and for the
training of trainers.
Component 2: Access to Education, US$ 193.3 million (IDA: US$ 36.4 million)
Objective. The objective of this component is to increase coverage and equity of education by expanding
primary and secondary school enrollment, mainly in rural areas, with special emphasis on enhancing the
participation of girls and boys living in underserved regions.
2.1    School and classroom construction and rehabilitation (US$ 177.2 million total; IDA US$ 31.8
million). The goal of this subcomponent is to expand primary and secondary places, with a focus on the
northern and central regions and rural areas, and on accessibility for girls. To help meet its goals for
greater access, Mozambique aims to build about 12,000 primary classrooms over the next five years.
MINED's construction targets are based on regional priorities defined according to projections of student
population growth, the accessibility of sites, current construction activity, and population density. In the
proposed scenario, 87% of the classrooms would be in rural areas, and the provinces with highest priority
would be Cabo Delgado, Manica, Nampula, and Niassa. The final selection of sites would primarily be
based on actual enrollment ratios in the regions and further supported by the results of school mapping,
(see institutional development component below). The construction program will support the
Government's decision to unify primary education into a single seven grade cycle.
Communities will build many of these schools through a Community School Program (CSP). The basic
principle underlying the design of the CSP is to maintain and strengthen the ability of communities to build
their own schools while improving the functionality and lifespan of the structures. Under this program,
future primary school construction by MINED will increasingly involve the participation of communities in
the planning and implementation of projects; more sustainable construction technology; and decentralized
management of the delivery process. MINED will build secondary school classrooms, houses, and
dormitories through traditional procurement methods. Annex 13 discusses the school building and
rehabilitation program in detail.
The program will finance (a) the construction of about 12,000 primary school classrooms and related
latrines and water supply; (b) the construction of about 2,100 staff houses for primary schools and 250 for
lower secondary schools; (c) construction of 25 lower secondary schools and 4 upper secondary schools
and dormitories; (d) rehabilitation of 15 existing lower secondary schools and 12 dornitories; (e) related
furniture and equipment; and (f) the costs of building design and supervision.
2.2    Girls' education initiatives (US$ 8.9 million total; IDA US$ 4.3 million). Socioeconomic,
geographical and cultural constraints cause great variance in girls' enrollment and completion across
regions. To encourage the enrollment and retention of girls at all levels of the educational system, the
program will provide scholarships for girls in upper primary and secondary schools, which will also serve
to increase the pool of female candidates for the teaching service; support an annual national award for best



Annex 2
Page 6 of 8
stories written with women as protagonists ("Women in Words"); introduce initiatives targeted at low-
income families and particularly girls, through the Caixa Escolar system; introduce changes in teacher
training programs to develop a school environment that is sensitive to gender issues; and increase the
capacity for central and provincial coordination of gender initiatives by establishing and training a Gender
Unit at the national level and in each province. Activities in other subcomponents will also benefit girls,
including the expansion of nonformal education, the provision of safe boarding facilities for female
students, and the unification of lower and upper primary schooling.
The program will finance (a) prizes and fellowships for girl students; (b) craft materials; (c) social studies
and surveys; (d) training of trainers for gender sensitivity courses; (e) evaluation visits; (f) gender-related
workshops, seminars and studv tours; (g) learning materials and modules; and (h) the costs of establishing
national and provincial Gender Units. including equipment, furniture and operating costs.
2.3    Nonformal education (US$ 5.5 million; IDA US$0.3 million). The program will promote the
establishment of nonformal education initiatives as alternative ways of providing literacy and other
education to girls, school dropouts, street children and adults. The Government is now developing a
strategy and will set targets after it is finalized MINED will continue to explore the application of new
technologies in the education system. in order to offer educational opportunities to the greatest possible
number of Mozamnbicans The program will provide books to local libraries and support the activities of
cultural centers.
The program will finance (a) technical assistance, training and printing costs to develop literacy courses for
women and girls, and the costs of monitoring and evaluating the program-; (b) training of trainers in
literacy. (c) technical assistance and funds for a rural credit and microenterprise scheme; (d) modules,
supervision and evaluation of courses for school dropouts; (e) materials, supervision and evaluation of
vocational training for students without access to school; (f) training, library materials, equipment and
furniture for cultural centers, and (g) training, technical assistance, and related equipment and vehicles for
adult literacy courses.
2.4    Special education (USS 1.6 million total; no IDA funding). The program will develop a strategy
for providing education to children with special needs by integrating them into regular lower primary
education. A model will be tested in four schools. The program will also develop training programs and
teaching tools for special education teachers, as well as strengthen and widen the special education schools
network.
The program will finance (a) training, fellowships, course development and related equipment costs for
establishing a model in special education, (b) training of teachers; and (c) rehabilitation and expansion of
special education schools.
Component 3: MINED Institutional Capacity, US$ 325.4 million (IDA: US$ 8.3 million)
Objective. The main objective of this component is to modernize and strengthen the education sector
management, a necessary condition to achieve the education plan's objectives to expand access and improve
quality. It will reinforce MINED's capacity to set policies and guidelines for the sector and improve the
education system's capacity for delivering education services effectively and efficiently.
Capacity building is integral to activities in all components, which fund activities such as the training of
school directors, upgrading for the ZIPs and the training of inspectors. Physical improvements, including
computenzation and associated training, will support these efforts. With the assistance of the Swedish



Annex 2
Page 7 of 8
government (ASDI), MINED has already begun implementing an overall institution building program. It
will support (i) development of management systems; (ii) development of policy and planning; (iii)
development of project management; (iv) improvement of MINED's financial management system;
(v) improvement of MINED's human resources system; and (vi) development of a public relations function
within MINED. The Ministry has already held a management seminar for provinces and districts to
familiarize them with the ESSP, to incorporate them into the planning process, and to begin discussion on
management issues. Additional seminars will continue to prepare the way for organizational development
and capacity building in the central MINED as well as the provinces and districts.
3.1    Organizational structure and decentralization (US$ 318.5 million total, including MINED
salaries; IDA US$ 4.1 million). This subcomponent will help MINED achieve the goals of the education
program, support the decentralization of functions, and establish new ways to deliver services. MINED
will test and develop new processes, procedures and systems during the first years of program
implementation. Implementation will be based on the review of MlNED's organizational structure at the
central and provincial levels.
This subcomponent will address the changes needed in MINED by adjusting its organizational structure as
needed and revising the legal framework of the education sector to support education activities at the
provincial and school levels. The program will improve MINED's (central and subnational units')
information, supervision, communication, and evaluation and monitoring systems. To better target ESSP
funding, the Ministry will also develop a school mapping system. MINED will revise teachers' career plan
and the performance incentive system for teachers, principals, supervisors and technical staff. As the
foundation for decentralization, MINED will establish a staff development program to improve the
technical capabilities of management, technical/pedagogical, and administrative staff to support reforms.
Training and technical assistance will help MINED develop program management capacity within central
and provincial directorates.
The program will finance (a) seminars on education management for national, provincial and district
officers; (b) seminars and technical assistance on gender and education; (c) courses on education planning
and administration; (d) equipment, software and technical assistance for school information systems,
including school mapping; and (e) the costs of MINED organizational development, including technical
assistance, transport and computer equipment, training, studies and operational expenses.
3.2 Policy and planning (US$ 1.6 million total; IDA US$ 1.0 million). This subcomponent aims to
create capacity within MINED at the central, provincial and district levels to analyze policy issues, monitor
and evaluate the education program on a yearly basis, and track and disseminate educational research in the
country. This subcomponent also aims to establish mechanisms to increase parent and community
participation. It will improve school autonomy and decentralization of the decision-making process at the
school and local levels to better address students' and communities' needs and expectations. The
subcomponent will also use various strategies to establish a new relationship between schools,
commnunities, and ZIPs. The program will support community and school participation by encouraging
formation of PTAs and increasing availability of financial resources and materials directly at the school
level (see subcomponent 1.6).
MINED has already carried out extensive capacity building to improve policy and planning at the center
and will now place increasing emphasis on the provinces and districts. Policy and planning development
are integral to efforts in organizational development and decentralization (see above), so key program costs
(e.g., information systems and school mapping) are included under subcomponent 3.1.



Annex 2
Page 8 of 8
3.3    Financial Management (US$ 3.1 million total; IDA US$ 2.2 million). This subcomponent will
provide crucial support for the decentralization effort by establishing systems of financial management for
all levels and providing training for staff at all levels. The program will introduce program budgeting for
the MINED at the central, provincial, district and school budgets.
The program will finance (a) computers and other related equipment and furniture for MINED center and
provinces; (b) technical assistance in financial management, audits, procurement and computer operations;
and (c) training for national and provincial finance and administration staff
3.4    Monitoring and Evaluation (US$ 2.2 million; IDA US$ 1.0 million). The ESSP is a
comprehensive strategic program for the sector and will be implemented as a rolling plan. For this reason,
monitoring and evaluation of the impact of investments on the education system and learning outcomes are
crucial. The ESSP-financed studies will include an analysis of education costs and financing, provide
detailed guidance on capacity building, and prepare a strategy for improving quality and efficiency at higher
education.
MINED will monitor and evaluate the program within the structure set up for implementation and detailed
terms of reference for key units within this structure. Monitoring and outcome indicators (see Annex 1)
will allow MINED to measure progress and will form the basis for joint supervisions with other donors.
Each unit involved with implementation will develop instruments for monitoring its respective component,
while the Planning Unit and the DAF will consolidate and analyze statistical, financial, and physical data on
the rate of implementation. The new PMR system will provide information on output indicators such as
expenditures, units built and number of teachers trained.
The program will finance the costs of studies and other information gathering and analysis, including the
related costs of technical assistance, training, and equipment.
Component 4: Technical Education Strategy, (US$ 42.1million; IDA US$ 0.5 million)
Objective. The program aims to make technical education more relevant and responsive to the needs of the
labor market and the public and private sector. The Government will prepare a strategy for the subsector,
which will form the basis for assistance during the second phase of the current program.
Activities. The ESSP includes notional estimates for developing the technical and vocational system
during the program period, with strong private sector involvement. Donors have expressed a willingness to
help. However, support through the ESSP will be conditional on devising an overall strategy for this
subsector that addresses its weaknesses. Following further study, the Government and donors would reach
agreement in 1999 on a way forward. IDA will fund the preparation of a strategy study on the reform of
the technical education system under the ongoing Capacity Building - Human Resources Development
Project. Once the basic education program is well underway and the strategy for vocational and technical
education is agreed, the Government and its partners can turn their attention not only to vocational and
technical education but also to the higher education system.

MOZAMBIQUE
Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP)
Economic and Financial Analysis
Links of ESSP to Country Assistance Strategy and HIPC Initiative
The HIPC Initiative approved 31 March 1998 and the latest Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) agree that
human development and education are central for further sustained economic growth and social
development in Mozambique- Assistance delivered under the HIPC Initiative includes general budgetary
relief as well as IDA's interim relief through grant financing1 of the Education Sector Strategic Plan,
1999-2003 (ESSP). This assistance will support not only the expansion of education infrastructurc but
also reinforce the sustainability of associated recurrent cost financing. Fiscal sustainabilitv and increased
internal funding are essential in order to meet the benchmarks set in the HIPC and CAS documents. Under
these benchmarks, by 2000 the primary gross admission rate will reach 80%/86%, intcrnal efficiency will
improve through a reduction of repetition rates from 33% to 27/o-29%, and drop-out rates will decline
from 8% to 5/o-6%. Reaching these benchmarks is critical if economic growth is to be sustained, enough
graduates are to enter the labor market, and the financial viability of the ESSP program is to improve.
Sector Work Supporting ESSP
The draft Education Sector Expenditure Review recommends that (i) expansion of access be targeted at
provincial and district levels to address inequities in access for rural populations and particularly girls, (ii)
internal efficiency be improved because it is one of the most critical factors determining the success of a
short to medium-term program for human resource development. (iii) social awareness of education and
commnunity involvement be strengthened, (iv) local contractors, artisans and local materials be used more in
school construction, and (v) learning achievements be improved by adapting the curriculum to the local
environment, improving management capacity at the school and central levels, and upgrading teachers'
qualifications through pre- and in-service training programs. A full cost and financing study following up
on, and updating the results of the draft Public Expenditure Review (PER) and the results of the recent
Household Survey will be prepared in 1999.
Also addressing these issues are a number of studies prepared for the Education Scctor Stratcgic Program
1998-2003 including: (i) Mainstreaming Gender; (ii) the Teacher Education Reform Project proposal:
Processes, Content and Structure of the 10+1 14 1 Model; (iii) National Policy on Textbook and Learning
Materials; and (iv) the Financial Plan. In addition, the World Bank/EDI supported the preparation of the
report "Benefits of Learning." The report analyzed the linkagc between cducation inputs and outputs and
found a significant correlation between learning outcomes and the availability of textbooks, good school
management, qualified teachers, frequent inspections and social awarcness (by parents and communities).
A working document on institutional assessment, "A Review of Management and Administration within
Ministry of Education," was prepared in 1997. Finally, a paper by Mozambican researchers titled "Human
Capital and Social Welfare in Mozambique" (Oct. 1998) which analyzed the result of the 1996-97
Household Survey, was consulted.
IA subsequent decision has been for the Bank to contribute to ESSP financing by means of an IDA Credit.



Annex 4
Page 2 of 14
Economic and Financial Analysis
The analysis conducted for the ESSP consists of (i) rationale for public intervention, (ii) cost-benefit
analysis, including sensitivity analysis, analysis of internal efficiency, and benefit incidence analysis, (iii)
fiscal analysis, (iv) analysis of cost-effectiveness, including alternatives.
Rationale for Public Intervention
There are both efficiency and equity grounds for additional public intervention in primary and secondary
education in Mozambique.
Massive empirical research in countries covering the entire spectrum of social and economic conditions
shows that returns on education investment are strongly positive, and typically rcturns for basic education
are higher than for upper middle and higher education. Mozambique is no exception to this rule as shown
in the next section on cost-benefit analysis.
The theoretical foundation for these empirical findings is that basic education (complete primary in low
income countries, lower secondary in middle income countries) is a principal pre-condition for sustained
economic growth. Basic education increases labor productivity in existing jobs, it enables the labor force
to move more easily to new jobs associated with higher levels of economic development, it is the
prerequisite (for a segment of the population) for advancement to middle level and higher level professions.
it induces reductions in undesirably high fertility rates- it helps improve the health status of the population.
it builds up a strong national identity via the spread of a common language and common cultural values,
and it increases social cohesion by demonstrating to disadvantaged population strata that they also belong
in the social mainstream.
With regard to equity questions, the recent Household Survey revealed that: (a) only 17.4% of all
Mozambicans 18-65 years old have completed five years of education (the completion rate for girls is
10.4% and for the poor 13.9%); (b) the highest attainment for children 7-1 1 years old is only 1.17 years of
education, (c) the main reasons for not attending primary schools in rural areas are the cost of education,
children's work (opportunity costs) and absence of a school in the village, and (d) only 49.2% of
Mozambicans 7-11 years old are enrolled in school, with only 39% of girls and 41.7% of the poorest
enrolled in the rural areas. These numbers demonstrate the urgency of improving both attainment and
achievements and increasing access to quality cducation for girls and the poorest, particularly in
underserved rural areas.
Private provision of primary education has increased by more than 500% sincc 1991, from about 7.000
students enrolled into private primary schools to about 40,000 students. Also, the number of schools not
receiving public funding as well as schools run by NGOs andlor religious organizations are increasing.
However, in absolute terms private education only covers about 2% of total enrollments in lowcr primary
education. Furthermore, all private schools are concentrated in urban areas, mainly Maputo, whereas the
need for expansion of primary education is mainly in rural arcas. Few parents can afford the fees charged
by private schools, and private lending for education is not commonplace. This situation suggests that
Government intervention and financing are needed.



Annex 4
Page 3 of 14
Cost-Benefit Analysis
A cost-benefit analysis has been carried out to evaluate the expected returns on the investment program.
Two computations have been applied in estimating the private and social internal rate of return (IRR): (I)
A simple rate of return calculation describing the current system in 1997, and (2) a cohort flow model for
computing the IRR of the proposed program. Finally, to underscore the proposed program's objectives
there is a brief discussion of the structure of the system and of ESSP's impact on poverty.
In both IRR calculations it is assumed that the market wage increase, as a proxy for the marginal product
of the accumulation of human capital, is nil after only two years of primarv schooling. In other words,
most children leaving the school system after two years will not be able to acquire higher paid jobs or
improve productivity. The cost to the pupil is the sum of costs for school materials and fees as it is
assumed that the opportunity cost from lost work is marginal for young children. Public sector financed
costs are used for computing the social rate of return.
Simple Rate oReturn Analysis. A simple intemal rate-of-return calculation2 is used to cstimate the
retums to primary education for grades 1, 5 and 7. The simple IRR calculation shows a negative private
and social IRR after two years of schooling of -0.8% and -4.0%, respectively (Table 1). This finding is
consistent with the reason given by the poorest rural households for not attending school (1997 Household
Survey, IAF), namely high direct costs. On the other hand, for a student completing grade 7 the private
and the social IRR are positive, rising to 18.5% and 17.3% respectively.

The cohort flow model estimates the private and the social IRR by completion of grade 7 to be 25. 1% and
11.5% respectively  under the proposed program, before improvements to internal efficiency. This means
that by completing primary education (grade 7). a person's lifetime earnings would increase bv about
25.1%. While the welfare gain to societv of the ESSP program for primar\ education w-ould be an
estimated 11.5%. These results do not take into account the economic gains resulting from improvements
in productivity and other externalities (child and maternal mortality rates and fertility rates) As can be
seen, the SSA average is about twice as high.
Improving Internal EIfficiency. As argued in the stmpic IRR calculation, the IRR to any investment in
Mozambique's education sector that does not improvc currcnt coMpletion rates is negative at the lowcr
grades. The reason for this is that the high repcrttion and drop out rates lead to a very high ratio of
enrollment to number of graduates. causing all costs. pUbbhc and private, to be very high oin a per graduatc
basis. Because repetition rates are high, students that do graduate takc vcry long to do it. This delay
postpones the time when their incomes rise, and it im-crcascs opportunity costs
It is important to note that the program will not only increase the numbcr ol students entering the sy stem
but its objective is also to improve intemal effic-cncy by 5)%. primaril through improvements to the
quality of education and elimination of incomplete schools As a consequence, thousands more w ill rcmain
in the system until completion. This improvement implies both an increase in prospective lifetime wages
for these students who would otherwise repeat or drop-out, as w-ell as a reduction in costs per graduate
The increase in the number of graduates that would rcsult from a reduction in repetition and drop-out rates
Is illustrated in Table 3. Even with a small 10% reduction in repetition and drop-out rates, the increase in
the number of graduates would bc substantial.

other words only every fifth student or less completes EP 1. Less than 6% complete full primary education
(EP2). The situation is even more serious with regard to the number of students completing lower
secondary education (ESG I). Only about 0.5 % of all students beginning school complete the 10th grade.
In other words; only one out of every 200 students completes lower secondary education. As mentioned
already low promotion rates, high drop-out rates and high number of repeaters combined with low,



Annex 4
Page 6 of 14
although improving, quality of instructional inputs and high opportunity costs contribute to this. For about
40% of the rural population high opportunity costs and direct costs were given as the main reason for not
attending school in the recent Household Survey.
The low levels of promotion and attainment can, in addition to high costs and inadequate quality, also be
explained by (i) parents/households uncertainty about the benefits of learning, and (ii) incomplete schools
(schools not offering a full five or seven grades cycle) or absence of a school.
On the issue of parents uncertainty regarding the benefits of education, this is closely linked to the quality
of the education given and the costs to be borne by the parents. At the moment it appears that at least for
the poorest parents, the costs outweigh the benefits. This is another reason the proposed program will
focus on strengthening the qualitv of education and parents' participation in their children's education
On the issue of incomplete schools, this is a widespread phenomenon affecting about 30% of all children
enrolled in EP 1 and more than 50% in some provinces as can be seen in Table 5 below.

In 1996 about 30% of all schools were incomplete, thus not able to offer a full primary cycle. This
affected about 420,000 pupils enrolled in primary education. In the northern provinces almost half of the
children enrolled could not complete the fifth grade for the same reason. The main reasons for the existence
of incomplete schools are the lack of physical space and classrooms and the very high enrollment rates into
the first and second grades. This suggests the need to target existing schools by building additional
classrooms as proposed under the program.
The absence of nearby schools is another reason for low completion rates in Mozambique, particularly in
the rural areas. In the Household Survey 32.3% of the rural children who did not have a school in their
village were not attending school because of the distance to the nearest school. This supports the
The 1997 Benefits of Learning Study, correlating education inputs to learning achievement clearly identified
"parents and the community awareness of the importance of education" as a contributing effect to learning
outcomes.



Annex 4
Page 7 of 14
program's focus on concentrating more than 80% of the school construction in remote or underserved areas
without access to education.
Benefit Incidence Analysis - Impact on Poverty
The IRR analysis has provided us with some indication of the priorities that should be considered by the
Government in improving intemal efficiency, namely reducing repetition and drop-out rates. However, the
analysis did not explain why there has not been a higher demand for private education, if returns are as
high as indicated.
First, it appears that there is indeed a huge demand for primary education as witnessed by the fact that
practically all children-bo's and girls, in rural and in urban areas where schools are available-have
returned to school since the countrv returned to normal conditions following the 1992 peace agreement.
But because most families live on subsistence incomcs, thev ma' find it verv difficult to set aside the
means-particularly monetary-to pay for such services, particularly if only offered by a private provider
where the costs are likely to be higher. And if credit markets work imperfectly, privatization and self-
financing will be difficult to achieve.
From another point of view, although the IRR analysis calls for further Government intervention in primary
education, as proposed under the ESSP program. it does not explain whether the proposed expansion would
actually reduce poverty and reach the poor. Too often, public spending in education is geared to a
quantitative expansion. Without an improvement in the quality of education and attainment, the need and
demand for education services may very rapidly decline and therefore have a limited effect on reducing
6
poverty. This has been the case in a number of SSA countries.  An educational policy should ensure that
the demand for education is increased or, at a minimum, sustained by improving the quality of the services
and by reducing the effects on the poor and ultra-poor of any new policies. MINED has already taken one
such action, under the proposed program MINED will distribute free textbooks for grades I to 7. The
measurcment for an improved "quality package" is an increase in per student spending (unit costs).
Another measurement is expanded access for the poorest. All are integral parts of the proposed program.
Under the program, it is estimated that the unit costs for EP I students will increase by about 40%. The
program is focused on expanding primarv education to the most underserved and impoverished areas, thus
benefiting the lower income groups. Furthermore, by introducing complete schools (grades 1 to 7) more
children, particularly in the rural areas, will benefit from improved access to grades 6 and 7. A question
that has not been solved is whether the poorest and girls will actually use the services provided-and thus,
whether the benefits will outweigh the costs. This can only be done by ensuring that policies such as the
Caixa Escolar will assist the poorest. Under the ESSP program, studies will analyze the reasons why
underserved groups drop out or do not attend school, so that more appropriate policies can be designed.
Figure 2, based on the calculated unit costs for 19957 and the population, shows school enrollment and
income distribution by quintiles. The diagonal line represents an equal distribution of the public subsidy

(quality) and the affordability for the poorest is analyzed.

higher-income groups; the lines closest to the diagonal, or above, indicate policies that benefit the poor the
most. Figure 2 clearly reveals a common pattern. Whereas the lower primary enrollment (EP 1) is the
most pro-poor, but still below full equity, current university spending mainly benefits higher-income
groups. It is not surprising that the graph depicts such a skewed curve. Only about 5,000 students were
enrolled in university in 1994 (equivalent to a gross enrollment rate of less than 1%). And more than 90%
of the students enrolled in university came from the highest income group. Secondary education is equally
pro-rich, although not to the same extent as university education. The ESSP program will move up the
EP I and EP2 curves toward the diagonal as access is expanded and EP I unit costs rise. Through targeted
interventions, mainly scholarship programs for girls, there should be a shift in the curves for secondary
education as well.
