Fiscal Impact and Sustainability
The total programn for the five-year period 1999-2003 is costed at US$717 million. This amount covers
capital as well as recurrent expenditure for primary education (grades 1-7), secondary education (grades 8-
12) and technical education, but excludes expenditures on higher education and the effects of planned
public sector salary decompression on education costs. An additional expenditure of US$ 101 million and
US$ 100 million (for the period 1999-2003) is estimated for salary decompression and for higher education
respectively. Thus, the total projected cost (capital and recurrent) for the provision of education services at
all levels could amount to about US$918 million in 1999-2003, or US$818 million without higher
education.
The fiscal viability of education sector expansion plans can be checked by comparing the above figures to
macroeconomic projections worked out by Bank and IMF staff in collaboration with Mozambican
authoritics. The Government will have to allocate up to 16% of projected public expenditures (US$918
million over total expenditurcs of US$5,767 million) to meet the public sector financed costs of this
program (including capital investments and higher education) over the 1999-2003 period. When higher
education is not included in the calculation. this perccntage is reduced to 14%, still a significant increase
ovcr the 9% allocated in 1998. The Ministcr of Finance, in his letter of sector policy to the Bank, commits
the Government to allocate up to 21 % of public expenditures to the education sector by the year 2003. The
fiscal viability of the proposed program of education sector expansion is assured both in terms of the
country's economic prospects and the Govemment's commitment to the sector's development. Also,
education (including univcrsities) would absorb an average of 4.4% of GDP per annum in the period 1999-
2003, which is modest in view of the high percentage of school-age children and the need to rebuild
infrastructure badly damaged during the war.
Capital expenditures (excluding higher education) of the ESSP program would make up 14% of all public
capital expenditures, and recurrcnt expcnditures (including salary decompression, but excluding higher
education) will make up 17.5% of all public recurrcnt expenditures over the 1999-2003 period. At the end
of the period education, excluding higher education, will absorb 18.7% of total recurrent expenditures as
shown in Table 6 below. As a share in total salaries education salaries will remain constant at 41-42%.

Medium Term Expenditure Framework These estimates are in broad agreement with the provisional
Medium Terrn Expenditure Framework developed by MPF with assistance from IMF and the World Bank.
During the life of the plan, a number of donors will progressively shift the focus of finance from specific
projects to the plan's components, with parallel shift from appraising individual activities to financing
shares of the sector strategy and move towards a sector wide approach (SWAP) and budgetary support.
During the joint assessment May 1998, most donors agreed to move towards a SWAP.
Cost-Effectiveness Analysis
Education Expenditures - Country Comparisons. During the 1994-1996 period, Mozambique devoted
about 2.9% of its GDP to education beforc grants aid to education. Only a few other countries in Sub-
Saharan Africa had a smaller proportion of GDP devoted to education the same year. Where provision of
primary education for all is estimated to require about 2% of a country's GDP, Mozambique devoted 3.1%
for the whole education sector on average, excluding foreign aid, from 1990 to 1997. It devoted less than
1% on primary education-far from the 2% rule of thumb. In 1992, Zimbabwe, South Africa, and
Swaziland allocated 9.1 %, 7.0 % and 6.0 % of their GDP to education, respectively. However, compared
to other low enrollment countries8 including Angola, Burkina Faso, Chad, Djibouti, Eritrea, Ethiopia,
Guinea, Guinea-Bissau, Mali, Niger, Rwanda and Senegal, Mozambique has matched their average of
about 3.0% in GDP (about 3.1% in GDP). At the same time, overall, Sub-Saharan Africa devoted 3.9% of
GDP to education. However, there are two concerns in relation to Mozambique: (i) its share in GDP may
equal other low enrollment countries, but the GDP per capita in Mozambique of US$140 is well below the
SSA averagc of US$450 or the above mentioned low enrollment countries of about US$300. Thus, in
absolutc terns Mozambique is lagging behind. And (ii) as a post-conflict country, the costs and the
investment needs in Mozambique for rebuilding infrastructure will require large investments in addition to
rcquired expenditures for expanding access.
D)istribution by Sub-sector levels. Figures 3 (a) and 3 (b) below illustrate the shift of internal recurrent
expenditures towards primary education in Mozambique (grade I to 7) from 1994 to 1997.
As Figure 3 shows, the share of primary education has increased from 38.5% to about 45.6% in 1997 with
a corrcsponding reduction in the share of Eduardo Mondlane University (UEM), technical vocational
training, teacher training and administrative costs. While the reduction in administrative costs, which
include provisions for districts and provincial offices is an encouraging trend, administrative costs continue
to take a substantial share (about 17%) of the overall budget. The consequence of this shift has been that
the unit cost for a primary student has recovered to the 1990 level of about US$13 per pupil while all other
sub-sectors unit costs have been declining.

Of the total expenditures for primary. sccondary and technical education, 74% goes to salaries and 26% to
good and services. EP I salaries make up almost 60% of the total salary envelope. EP2 salaries account
for 1 1% of total salary envelope. The shares to ESG I and ESG2 have increased from 5. 1% and 1.6%,
respectively, in 1990 to 8% and 4% in 1997, respectively. Central administration (including the provinces)
has been reduced from 20% in 1990 to 12% in 1996. Tertiarv education makes up about 15% of the total
public recurrent expenditures on education. However. historically the higher education sector has received
significant external contributions. making up more than 50% of their expenditures.
By' year 2001, without an introduction of new programs for technical and vocational training and higher
education, administration will cover 12%, primary education 49%, secondary education 14%, teacher
training will double to 6%, technical and vocational training 4%, tertiary education 12% and others 3%
under the new program.

Cost-Effectiveness of ESSP
In order to provide an estimate of the effectiveness of the proposed program the following two tables 7(a)
and 7(b) (at the end of the annex) compare projections of students and graduates by level of education
when intemal efficiencv remains the same as in the recent past (Scenario 1). and when it is increased bv
100% (i.e. when repetition and drop-out rates are reduced by 50% as planned under the proposed
program-ESSP Scenario).
The two tables are based on preliminary data from the 1997 population census. which show that the actual
numbers for school-age groups are about 20% lower than those based on projcctions from the 1980
population census.
Tables 7(a) and 7kb) have been combined in Table 8 with regard to number of graduates bv level. In
addition. Table 8 shows numbers of graduatcs for the ycars 1996-1998, which are of course the same for
ESSP and Scenario I The last column shows "net" numbers of graduates for each level for 1996-2003,
i.*  those who rcmain in that level after deducting the graduates at the next higher level (the number of
i-ul!'cdeCs for lower and upper secondary arc added together) The last two lines show total expenditures
1996-2003 for ESSP and Scenario I Notc that expenditurcs for ESSP for 1999-2003 are higher than
th.;sc presented in the PAD, and they reflect earlier cost estimates. They are kept herc because they are
ccrisidered to be comparable to corresponding earls estimates of Scenario I (there are no updated cost
cstimates of Scenario 1).
T able 8 has been used to estimate the cost-effectiveness of ESSP by using the following simplifications:
First, because therc are no cost profiles of the system's graduates, i.e., there are no estimates of the stream
of the annual expenditures during a person's studies which are necessary for that person to graduate,
required expenditures have bccn approximated by those actually realized during the period that the
graduates arc produced. simplN cxtending the period as long as available data allow (in this case, 1996-
2003) in order to eliminate part of the bias. Second, because there is no cost breakdown by level of
education an approximation has been used to apply as weights the monthly costs of teachers for each level
(US$37 for EP1. US$ 78 for EP2, and US$ 159 for lower and upper secondary levels combined as there
are no detailed salarv estimates for these two levels).
Applying thcsc strong simplifying assumptions to the figures contained in Table 8, it can be shown that the
cost of ESSP would be US$ 1 .19 billion, if the relative costs of producing graduates at the various levels
under Scenario I remained the same. As the actually calculated cost of ESSP is US$998 million, the cost
savings of ESSP (mainly from increasing internal efficiency) are 19.2%. This is probably an underestimate
of the true savings (assuming of coursc that the planned improvements in internal efficiency are realized)
for two reasons: One, as mentioned earlier the latest cost estimates for ESSP are significantly less than the
figure in Table 8. And two, truncating the cost profiles excludes from the calculation the higher number of
future graduates associated with current levels of spending.

MOZAMBIQUE
Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP)
Financial Management
The salient features of the financial management system for the MINED Directorate of Administration
and Finance (DAF), as outlined in a Discussion Paper that was recently approved by MINED, are as
follows:
A    GENERAL
1. Financial Resources for ESSP
1.1    The principal financial resources for ESSP will consist of (a) The Government's budget for
education, (b) new funds from coopcrating partners, including IDA, to be expended through the new
ESSP Basket (please refer to 2 below), and (c) funds from any ongoing and any new "parallel" (non-
Basket) support from cooperating partners.
1.2    Developing a financial management system to fully capture the funds referred to in 1.1 above
will involve 2 phases as follows:
Phase I (Short to Medium 7erm): During this phase, (a) above will continue to be administered by
DAF and the provincial DPEs in accordance with existing Government procedures; (b) will be
administered by DAF in accordance with the systems and procedures outlined in this Appendix; funds
under (c) will also be captured in accordance with the systems and procedures outlined in this
Appendix.
Phase 2 (Medium to Long term): During this phase, following extensive consultation between all
Government ministries (MINED, MPF etc.) and, if deemed appropriate, a comprehensive study by an
external firm of management consultants, a fully integrated financial management system for
Government will be established.
2. ESSP Basket Funding
2.1    The principle of basket funding is that cooperating partners will contribute to a "common
basket (i.e. the ESSP Basket) instead of supporting their own individual projects. ESSP Basket
financial statements will be produced showing the expenditures financed from the ESSP Basket as
well as the sources of funds spent, i.e. Financial Statements will be produced on both disaggregated
(i.e. analyzed by cooperating partners) and consolidated bases.
3. MINED Directorate of Administration and Finance (DAF)
3.1    DAF's new financial management system (FMS) will support management in their
deployment of limited resources with the purpose of ensuring economy, efficiency and effectiveness in
the delivery of outputs required to achieve desired outcomes. The FMS should be capable of
producing relevant, understandable, timely and reliable financial information that should enable
management to plan, implement, monitor and appraise the Ministry's overall progress towards the
achievement of its objectives.



Annex 5
Page 2 of 7
3.2    The current structure of DAF was not designed to meet the objectives and programs as
described in the Education Sector Strategic Plan. Therefore, for DAF to fully deliver on its new and
expanded mandate, its financial management system will be strengthened and enhanced in line with the
key features presented in B below.
4. Loan Administration Change Initiative (LACI)
4. 1    The successful implementation of DAF's new FMS should facilitate the future introduction of
PMR-based disbursements. A financial management review of the program will be undertaken by a
World Bank financial management Specialist within 12 months of credit effectiveness to assess
progress. Meanwhile, for at least 12 months after credit effectiveness, existing disbursement
procedures will be followed.
B      KEY FEATURES
1. Financial Management Committee (FMC)
1. 1    A representative FMC (including MPF) wvill be appointed to review the Program's
Management Report every quarter. The report will comprise:
Financial statements, as discussed in paragraph i1.2 below. Members of the FMC will review and
approve quarterly and annual financial statements. they will also examine material variances between
budget/actual figurcs - seeking remedial action, as appropriate, within an agreed timeframe.
Program progress, i.e. Output Monitoring Report (OMR). The format and details of the OMR
will need to be developed. An important aspect of the OMR will be the accompanying narrative
interpreting the program s progress with agreed financial performance indicators and how costs to
date relate to those planned at appraisal, and its likely effect on the program at its completion.
2. Staffing
2.1    A Financial Controller/Deputy Financial Controller will be appointed to direct and guide the
financial management operations of ESSP.
2.2    Relevantly qualified/experienced accounting, administrative/procurement and support staff
will be appointed. Substantial investment in staff training will be required in: financial, management
and government accounting; information systems and computer applications; procedures relating to
utilization of funds, e.g. Special Accounts, statement of expenditure (SOEs), special commitments,
procurement etc. On the job coaching will be provided.
2.3    Appropriate logistical arrangements will be made for an enlarged DAF, e.g. fumiture and
equipment/computers, accommodation, telephones, fax, email, transport etc.



Annex 5
Page 3 of 7
3. Financial Procedures Manual/Internal Audit
3.1   Internal control procedures will be documented in a Financial Procedures Manual. The
distribution of IDA funds at the provincial/district level will be modeled on the Caixa Escolar (school
fund) system, as outlined in the Plano Operativo da Caixa Escolar.
The activities of the program will be periodically reviewed by the Internal Audit Unit at
MPF - Inspeccao Geral de Financas (IGF).
4. Information Technology
4.1    Information technology requirements for ESSP (hardware, software and training) will be
determined at the earliest opportunity. Following consultation with the external auditors, the
accounting system will be computerized as soon as is practically feasible using an integrated accounts
package.
4.2    Software selection will be influenced by: availability and user friendliness/language, technical
support and back-up, training facilities for program staff, costs, computer controls, technology
compatibility with the systems being used by MPF.
4.3    The Financial Controller/Deputy Financial Controller will provide support in the use of the
accounting software. Some extemal consultancy support may be required.
5. Planning and Budgeting
5.1    Counterpart funding will be approved in line with Government's budgetary process.
5.2    FMC will be responsible for budget preparation for utilization of ESSP Basket funds with, as
appropriate, the concurrence of cooperating partners. The Financial Controller, in consultation with
FMC, will be responsible for preparing the program's quarterly/annual cash flow forecast.
5.3    The budget will ultimately be approved by MPF in consultation with MINED.
6. Government Accounting - Cash Versus Accruals
6.1    The FMC will determine the basis of accounting that will be adopted.
7. Procurement of Goods, Works and Services
7.1    To facilitate control, it is advisable that each contract should receive funds from only one
external cooperating partner.
7.2    The Financial/Deputy Financial Controller and senior staff members will be conversant with
procurement procedures, as internal control issues and the incurring of liabilities are matters of
particular concern to the financial management function.



Annex 5
Page 4 of 7
8. Banking Activities
ESSP Basket
8.1    For the ESSP Basket, each cooperating partner will advance funds to a dedicated banking
account. Funds from the various cooperating partners would not be mingled prior to their utilization.
ESSP Basket financial statements will be produced showing the expenditures (according to the agreed
Chart of Accounts) financed from the ESSP Basket as wtell as the sources of funds spent, e.g. IDA,
Bilateral A, Bilateral B, etc.
Bank Accounts
8.2    The Government will keep the funds from IDA and each other donor in separate accounts but
will move toward common procurement, financial management and auditing procedures acceptable to
all partners. Financial statements will show ESSP basket expenditures as well as the source of the
funds. Donor agencies support the gradual transfer of funds to MINED through budget support as its
financial management capacities improve. For IDA and counterpart funding, DAF will maintain
accounting records with respect to the following three bank accounts: (a) a current account in meticais
into which the Government will deposit its counterpart funding; (b) a dollar-based Special Account
(SA); and (c) an IDA Loan/Credit Account. including meticais, dollar and SDR movements. In line
with good accounting practice, the accounting records for cach bank account will be reconciled
monthly by DAF with the bank statements for each of the 3 accounts upon their receipt. Technical
assistance (including a financial controller and a deputy controller) will support DAF. The program
will strengthen DAF to provide general financial management of the central Ministry directorates and
agencies, the provincial and district directorates. and schools.
8.3    The flow of IDA funds related to textbooks and school materials, grants to schools and
community school construction will be modeled on the existing Caixa Escolar (school fund) system,
which the Government established in 1989 to support education at the community level. This system
is now being used primarily for the procurement and distribution of learning materials. Under the
Caixa Escolar system, annual budgets and distribution plans are prepared at the district level in
collaboration with schools and communities, and approved by MINED and the Ministry of Planning
and Finance. Separate bank accounts (administered by a provincial treasurer) are established for each
province, and sub-accounts are established for districts with local capacity. Allocated funds are
distributed through the provincial directorates of planning and finance (DPPFs) to the DPEs (Caixa
Escolar accounts) for disbursement at the school level. The Caixa Escolar is audited annually.
Disbursement of IDA Funds
8.4    Disbursement from IDA will be made on the basis of incurred eligible expenditures. IDA will
make advance disbursement from the proceeds of the Credit by depositing into a Borrower-operated
Special Account to expedite program implementation. The advance to a Special Account is to be used
by the Borrower to finance IDA's share of program expenditures under the Credit. Another
acceptable method of withdrawing funds from the Credit is the direct payment method, involving
direct payments from the Credit to a third party for works, goods, and services upon the Borrower's
request. Payments may also be made to a commercial bank for expenditures against IDA special
commitments covering a commercial bank's Letter of Credit. IDA's Disbursement Letter stipulates a
minimum application value for direct payment and special commitment procedures. The Association
may require withdrawals from the Credit Account to be made on the basis of statements of



Annex 5
Page 5 of 7
expenditure for expenditures for: (i) works under contracts not exceeding $200,000 equivalent (ii)
goods under contracts not exceeding $100,000 equivalent, (iii) consultants' services (firms) under
contracts not exceeding $100,000 equivalent, (iv) consultants' services (individuals) under contracts
not exceeding $50,000 equivalent, and (v) operating costs and training under such terms and
conditions as the Association shall specify by notice to the Borrower.
ESSP will use a simplified Special Account disbursement procedure which allows decentralized
program implementation. The Government will open a Special Account in the Bank of Mozambique.
The Special Account will be operated by designated officials of MINED. The decentralization of
program accounts would be introduced initially on a pilot basis in 3 provinces. Following agreement
between the Government (Schools, DDEs, DPEs, DAF, DP, DPPF, MPF) and cooperating partners
on the annual work program and budget, procurement would be organized through the 3 DPEs in
accordance with ESSP's procurement procedures. Each of the 3 participating DPEs would operate 2
bank accounts as follows:
(a)    A current account in meticais into which counterpart funding by the Government would be
deposited on a replenishment basis -- with an opening 2 months, float and supported by monthly
replenishment requests of prior month expenses to DPPF.
(b)    A sub-account in dollars of the Maputo Special Account with, for example, Standard Totta
Bank of Mozambique, to which dollars funding from IDA would be deposited on a replenishment
basis - with an opening 2 months, float and supported by monthly replenishments of prior month
expenses.
8.5    The operation of the sub-special account would ease the burden on GOM of having to pre-
finance activities in the provinces where most program activities would be implemented. All payments
made from the sub-SA would have full supporting documentation including, if appropriate, evidence
of goods received. Both bank accounts would be reconciled monthly, and those reconciliation's should
be reviewed and approved by DPPF and DAF as part of the replenishment process.
8.6    Upon Credit effectiveness, MINED will submit a withdrawal application for an initial deposit
to the Special Account, drawn from the IDA Credit, in the amount agreed to in the development credit
agreement. Replenishment of funds from IDA to the Special Account will be made upon evidence of
satisfactory utilization of the advance, reflected in the SOEs and/or on full documentation for
payments above the SOE thresholds. Replenishment applications should be submitted regularly -
ideally every-month but not exceeding three month intervals. If ineligible expenditures are found to
have been made from the Special Account, the Borrower will be obligated to refund the same. If the
Special Account remains inactive for more than six months, the Borrower may be requested to refund
to IDA amounts advanced to the SA.
9. Withdrawals/Disbursements
9.1    At this juncture in the relatively embryonic stage in the development of DAF's new financial
management system, DAF is not yet ready for PMR-based disbursements, as discussed in the World
Bank's Loan Administration Change Initiative Handbook (LACI, September 1998). Thus, for at least
12 months after credit effectiveness, existing disbursement procedures should be followed. In due
course, the successful implementation of the new financial management system should facilitate the
future introduction of PMR-based disbursements.



Annex 5
Page 6 of 7
9.2    Meanwhile, procedures relating to withdrawals/special commitments, statements of
expenditures and Special Account, as outlined in the World Bank's Disbursement Handbook, will
apply. Regarding cooperating partners, similar procedures will be established
10. Fixed Assets, Construction and Inventories
10.1   A fixed assets register will be prepared. regularly updated and checked for capital
expenditure Regarding construction/capital work in progress. controls will be established over the
awarding of contracts as well as for ensuring that payments will be made only in respect of certified
work. Contract status reports will be prepared quarterly for consideration by the FMC.
10.2   An inventories management svstem, including distribution to the provinces, will be established
and procedures i1ll be documented in the Financial Procedurcs Manual.
11. Financial Reporting (Monthlv/QuarterlN and Annually)
Monthly Cash Reporting
11. t   In compliance w\ith Govemment reporting requirements, the Financial Controller will be
responsible for preparing a Monthly Return to MPF for consolidation into the National Accounts of
Mozambique.
Quarterly/Annually
11 .2 The financial statements, following determination by the FMC, are likelv to include:
A statement of sources and uses of funds by Credit category/by activity;
A balance sheet as at the reporting date.
Notes in respect of significant accounting policies and accounting standards adopted by management
when preparing the accounts.
Special account statement/reconciliation showing deposits and replenishments received, pavments
substantiated by withdrawal applications. interest that may be eamed on the account and the balance
at the end of the fiscal year.
SOE withdrawal schedule, listing individual withdrawal applications relating to disbursements by the
SOE Method, by reference number, date and amount,
A cash forecast for the next 2 quarters.
11.3  Indicative formats for Financial Statements are outlined in two World Bank publications:
Financial Accounting Reporting and Auditing Handbook (FARAH, January 1995) and The Loan
Administration Change Initiative Handbook (LACI, September, 1998).

12. External Audit
12.1   Relevantly qualified, experienced and independent auditors will be appointed on acceptable
terms of reference. The external auditor will also be expected to prepare a separate Management
Letter giving observations and commnents, and providing recornmendations for improvements of
accounting records, systems, controls and compliance with financial covenants.

MOZAMBIQUE
Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP)
Procurement and Implementation Arrangements
Procurement arrangements refer to the IDA financed share of the Program.
Organizational Structure
Central Level: The procurement under the program will be highly decentralized with the bulk of
the construction works and National Competitive Bidding (NCB) procurement delegated to the
provinces. The MINED center will still have a particular relevance for the implementation of the
program since delegation to provinces is still at a preliminary stage, and most of management
decisions have been so far taken exclusively by the center. Moreover, even if decentralization of
authority will be a major factor for the success of the program, the center will maintain the
important function to manage the overall program, assisting the provinces in acquiring the
necessary skills and providing them with both technical assistance and standard bid documents.
designs and specifications. In order to improve the performance by MINED Center, the
following steps will be completed before the start of the program: (i) an experienced Executive
Secretary has already been selected. He will lead a Secretariat and a nucleus of M[NED technical
experts and an experienced management consultant, (ii) two experienced procurement staff from
GEPE have been incorporated into MINED, and (iii) at least four members of the secretariat will
be trained at an advanced procurement management seminar to be held in March, 1999 in
Maputo. The center will carry out all ICB procurement and consultants selection under the
program, will coordinate activities at provincial level, and closely assist the provinces with
technical advice, standard documentation, construction plans and specifications. The center will
also coordinate with the Bank and other participating partners (donors) either directly or through
the Resident Mission.
Provincial Level: The implementation of the program will largely be decentralized to the
Provinces due to the nature of works spread over very large areas and the small amount of the
procurement contracts. The capacity of the MINED provincial offices is, however, very limited
due to lack of previous experience in implementation. In order to achieve results, and facilitate
the gradual transfer of authority to provincial, and eventually district level, only three pilot
Provinces will start managing procurement on the first year of the program. Each of the
provinces will establish an implementation unit at the provincial MINED offices, with a
minimum of 2 experienced staff capable to manage and carry out procurement. In addition, a
massive program of capacity building will be carried out at the start of the Program with at least 2
staff from each province participating to a procurement seminar on Bank procurement
procedures to be held in Maputo, in March 1999. The provincial MINED offices, with the
assistance by the Center. will carry out procurement of small works, fumiture. educational
materials, supervise the works and assist the rural districts in the activities under the Community
School Program (CSP). The MINED offices will have the option to delegate management and
supervision of works to external consultants. The priority actions to be taken by MINED at
provincial level are described in more detail below.
Procurement of Works
Construction of Classrooms and Rehabilitation: The vast program of construction works
under the program concerns mostly construction and rehabilitation of primary schools in single
units of 2-4 classrooms with an average cost of US$15-25,000 equivalent. The individual
classrooms will be built in rural areas, mostly as an addition to existing structures often at
considerable distance from the major provincial towns. Since packaging of these small structure



Annex 6
Page 2 of 7
into larger lots is impractical, these small construction works will not attract the interest of large
contractors given their remote location. Therefore, it will be necessary to resort in most cases to
the use of small, local contractors and to the simple procurement methods under the Community
School Program. The construction of classrooms under CSP is essential to guarantee that a
sufficient number of classrooms will be built each year (see also Annex 13 for more details). The
community will receive funds for the purchase of construction material (bricks. cement. steel
slabs etc.) and will have the options to either build the classrooms for themselves or resort to
NGOs and small contractors. The value of the individual contracts is not expectcd to be higher
than $50,000 equivalent and may be procured under lump sum. fixed price contracts awarded on
the basis of quotations received from three qualified contractors. Standard models of classrooms.
of a design suitable to local conditions, have been prepared by MINED center and will be
furnished to the provincial offices of MINED. Supervision and technical advice for the
construction will be furnished by technical supervisors from the MINED provincial offices whlic
will visit each of the constrmction sites on a rotational basis.
Construction of Primary Schools and School Complexes: The capacity of commlluniities and
local contractors will not be sufficient to cope with the large number of classrooms to be built
under the program, and they would have insufficient technical skills for the construction of new
school complexes (embryonic units. constituted bv an average of 4 classrooms. administration
and storage rooms, and I to 3 small houses for teachers). Contracts for these stnuctLres have an
estimate value of $50,000 to $100.000 equivalent. The contracts therefore will be widely
advertised in order to attract national contractors under National Competitive Bidding (NCB)
method of procurement. Even if nationally advertised, contracts located in the remote areas of
the northern and western provinces may not attract competition from the relatively small, Maputo
based, Mozambican constnictioni industry. The demand for construction would. however, favor
the use of local sub-contractors and stimulate the growth of a more active construction industrV.
NCB will be based mostlyv on national procurement regulations. whichi were reviewed by the
Bank and considered adequate. Standard bid documents in Portuguese language for use in NCB
contracts will be drafted by MINED before the start of the implementation and will be reviewed
by the Bank before effectiveness. The first 5 contracts of each province and any contract higlher
than US$200.000 will be subject to IDA prior review.
Construction of Secondary Schools and Technical Colleges: Packaging of works under larger
contracts may be possible for groups of schools to be built near main provincial towns. and
whenever larger structures (such as secondary and pre-university schools) will also be built in
order to attract intemational contractors. Advertising of procurement notices (GPNs and SPNs)
in Development Business will be carried out at least once a year, and intemnational contractors
will be allowed to bid for anv contract both below and under the threshold of US$500,000
established for International Competitive Bidding (ICB). Bank procedures for ICB contracts and
standard bid documents will be used throughout the program and contracts subject to prior
review.
Procurement of Goods
Procurement of Nationally Available Goods: A great variety of goods and equipment will be
purchased under the program. The purchase of simple construction material such as cement bags,
bricks, construction stones. stcel roofing etc. will be carried out directly by the communities (or
via some of the other options described in Annex 13), under procedures to be specified in a
manual for community participation works. Funds for the purchase will be probably obtained at
district level through the mechanism of the existing Caixa Escolar (School Fund). Small
contracts for educational materials, such as chalk, stationery and books and for furniture and
building fixtures. such as doors and window frames, will be purchased locally either by National



Annex 6
Page 3 of 7
Shopping (NS) or NCB, depending on the amount of the contract and local conditions. NS will
be limited to a maximum amount of $20,000 equivalent for individual contract and up to an
aggregate amount of US$250,000 equivalent. The MINED provincial offices wvill package
individual contracts into larger ones on the basis of yearly schedules for each province, and will
carry out advertising at local and national level and procurement on the basis of standard bid
documents and specifications fumished by MINED central. Each individual contract estimated to
cost more than US$200.000 will be purchased by MINED center through ICB method of
procurement and the procedures specified in the Bank Procurement Guidelines. January 1995
edition. revised September. 1997.
Procurement of Goods Not Available at National Level: Each individual contract above the
threshold of US$200.000 and contracts for goods not available locally, suchl as vehicles and
computers, will be procured by MINED center through ICB and large packages at the beginninlg
of each fiscal yvear. Contracts wvill be advertised in Development Business at least once a year.
SPNs will be published in national newspapers of wide circulation. IDA procurement procedures
and SBDs will be used tlhrouglhout. All ICB contracts will be subject to prior revicw. Basic
office equipment such as desk computer. printer. fax machine and I (occasionally 2 ) vehicle(s)
for the pilot provinces may be purchased from the International Agency Procurcment Services
Offices (IAPSO) of UNDP in order to expedite program implementation at provincial level.
International Shopping (IS) and Limited International Bidding (LIB) procurement procedures
may be required for individual contracts below the respective thresholds of US$50.000 and
US$100,000 equivalent. for the purchase of specialized goods, such as textbooks. software and
equipment. up to the aggregate limit of US$250,000 and US$1,000,000 respectively. as specified
in the Development Credit Agreement (DCA). Direct Contracting will be used only in
exceptional cases, wvhen no competition is expected such as for the purchase of proprietary
software and small quantities of spare parts for existing equipment.
Procurement of Consulting Services: Consulting services for TA, capacity building and
training constitute an essential part of the Program. Consultants, both national anid intemational,
will be needed at central level to reinforce the capacitv of MINED to manage the Program and
coordinate the numerous activities tinder the program. Provincial MINED offices and districts
will also need technical support for managenment. procurement, supervision, marketing of
textbooks and technical advice to coimmiiiuinities and districts. MINED center will have the
responsibility for drafting TORs for the consulting assignments and selecting suitable consultants.
MINED center will also have to draft standard TORs for the most common assignments for use of
the provinces. IDA procedures w ill be followed throughout and consulting services expected
under the program w ill be advcrtised in Developmcnt Business prior to implementation and
updated each y ear.
Selection Methods: The mcthlod of selection is described in the DCA. The Quality and Cost
Based Selection (QCBS) metlhod will be general used under the program though other methods
such as the selection by qualifications (e.g. for groups of "animators" in rural areas) or by fixed
budget in case of periodically repeated services for which estimates are clearly determined such
as administrative tasks or auditinig assignme1cnits. Training contracts will follow the same selection
procedures used for conisultinig assignmients, though in special cases such as attendance to
specialized courses. a particular Institution mav be designated. Sole Source selection of
consultants will be made only! in exceptionally circumstances, with IDA prior agreement and as
provided for in the DCA. The selection procedures for contracts above US$100,000 and the
TORs of each assignment will be subJect to prior review.
Individual consultants will be selected under the procedures specified in Section V of the Bank
Procurement Guidelines for the use of consultants, on the basis of their qualifications through



Annex 6
Page 4 of 7
comparison with other candidates qualifications. The TORs of each assignment and the selection
procedures for individual consultants for contract values above the threshold of US$50.000
equivalent will be subject to IDA prior review.
Implementation Manuals and Standard Documents: Bank Procurement Guidelines and SBDs
will be used for all ICB procurement. Standard bid documents, contracts and specifications will
be used for NCB procurement at provincial level. A manual of simplified construction
techniques and procurement procedures for use of the communities and NGOs w-ill be used at
district level. MiNED center wvill have the responsibility for the drafting and distribution of
manuals and standard documents to the provinces. The provincial MINED officcs Nwill have to
maintain a complete library of the documentation for use of the districts.
Prior and Post Review of Procurement: Contracts for works under ICB and NCB contracts
above US$200.000 and the first 5 NCB contracts of any value carried out bv eaclh province w ill
be subject to IDA prior review. Similarly, ICB contracts for goods and any contract over
US$100,000 and the first 5 NCB contracts of any value carried out at provincial level wvill also be
subject to prior review. LIB. IS and requests for the use of direct contracting will have to be
approved by the Bank. The review process for ICB would include advertising in Devclopmncit
Business, the review of the SBDs aid specifications. tendering procedures and evaluation reports.
and contract signature. In addition. anv standard bid document for NCB. the malnuals of
procedures, the standard design and specifications for classrooms and embryo structures to be
used throughout the program will be reviewed and approved by the Bank before their release. All
other procurement will be subject to post-review of a representative sample of contracts on a
yearly basis. Third party verificationi of procurement actions at provincial and district level may
also be carried out and financed unider the program. Information to be revieced may include
compliance with procurement procedures, schedules of implementation. cost comparisoni and
contractual claims. TORs for all consulting assignments and training contracts. and contracts for
consulting services above the threslhold of US$100.000 (for firms) and $50.(0() for individual
consultanits will be subject to prior review.
Procurement Schedules: Procuremiient under ESSP will be a complex activity requiring strategic
planning and monitoring skills. MINED center will have the responsibility to coordinate and
maintain a general implemlentationi schedule for all the Program. Each of the Provincial offices.
however, will be required to maintain a bar chart of the procurement activities under their
administration. Delays in construction or delivery of equipment will be immediatcly noted and
contractors/suppliers reminded of their obligations. Programs for ICB procuremenit during the
first two years of implementation will have to be prepared by MINED prior to implementation.
Table I and Tahle 2 at the end of the annex show the prociurement methods and the allocation o*f
Crecit proceeds.
Harmonization of Donors Procurement Procedures: The ESSP has attracted the interest of a
large number of donors presently supporting the education sector in Mozambique. Harnonization
of procurement and disburscltlemet procedures is feasible under the Program and has been subject
of a detailed study by IDA (see Mr. F. Sarno "Donors Harnonization Report for ESSP and
PROAGRI in Mozambiquc"). It is expected that some of the donors will agree on common
thresholds for procuremenit under NCB the use of post-review and of reporting formats. Donors
may also agree on the use of standard documents and procedures for advertising, evaluating and
awarding the contracts. IDA proccdures and documentation will be followed for all ICB
procurement. GOM procurclllemet regulations may be used for NCB contracts, provided that IDA
procurement regulations would prevail in case of conflict.

MOZAMBIQUE
Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP)
Curriculum Development
(Excerptfrom the Government of Mozambique/UNESCO Project Document)
Project MOF/0096102
From Section A, para. 1. Introduction
Though the poor efficiency of primary education cannot be attributed to the aims and management
of the curriculum. it may be said that these do not facilitate improving the system's efficiency, nor
permit reasonable gains from time devoted to education.
When the National Education System was introduced, primary education was organized into a first
level (EP l) lasting five vears and a second level (EP2) lasting two years. EP I was planned to have
one teacher per class. while EP2 was to be organized by subjects. with each one taught by a
different teacher.
Parallel to the introduction of the SNE, materials for pupils and teachers in all classes were
developed year by vear. The course materials were prepared in accordance with the specific
political principles and pedagogical and didactic concepts of the time. They were printed and
reprinted without the possibility of revising them.
In 1996 plans and programs for primary education prepared on the basis of the old teachers'
manuals were published. Also in 1996. revised editions of the pupils' textbooks were published,
containing alterations to some aspects of the political principles in order to make their messages
appropriate to the new scenario of peace and the transition from one-party govemment to a multi-
party parliamentary democracy. The choice and organization of the content which seemed less
bound to the old political principles were not changed, nor were the pedagogical and didactic
concepts.
Various programs of initial training and upgrading of teachers were established to meet the needs of
the expansion of primary education, of different lengths (ranging from six years of basic education
plus one of specific training to ten years of education plus two of specific training). There are
programs for EPI on the one hand and EP2 on the other, with no scheme for either making them
establishing equivalencies or coordinating between them.
When the SNE was introduced, a strategy for the educational system was designed based on ZIPs
(Zones of Pedagogical Influence), with core schools promoting the interchange of experiences and
the qualitative improvement of education. The war inhibited the possibility of advancing with a
process of decentralizing curriculum management that would encourage the ZIPs. The lack of
guidelines resulted in them becoming progressively weaker.
The difficult national situation limited access to intemational debates on pedagogy, and also
hampered the development of enough applied research to stimulate the solution of certain specific
problems, such as improving methodologies for teaching Portuguese as a second language, and
introducing mother tongues as an educational tool and - in experimental projects - content. It has
recently been possible to carry out some work towards this, but it is not yet sufficiently developed
to enable itto be generalized.



/'7.P   N  1.1
2. Previous or current technical assistance
However, even in the worst of conditions Mozambique managed to maintain a certain level of
institutional capability in the field of curricula. This is concentrated in EIDE (Na1ional inst.lttc for
Educational Development).
INDE was established in 1978 as a specialized institution subordinated to the Miriist'x'-f ErAien.
with intellectual and administrative autonomy.
It had the responsibility for preparing the teaching plans and programs, the textbooks fbr pr:  auPls
the teachers' manuals.
At the beginning of the 1980s it received support from a UNESCO/LUNDP program, t. om. P
German Democratic Republic and from researchers from other countries.
Later on the international contacts wvere discontinued and all responsibilitv for curriculum
development was removed from the institution. INDE then concentrated its efforts on research.
In 1989 SIDA (Swedish International Development Agency) financed a project evaluating scholol
books in the context of their end use. which provides a substantial base of ernpirical data. fmtn
which to take on a process of curriculum transformation.
As a continuation of that project SIDA is currently undertaking a capacity building projifct for
curriculum research. with the assistance of the Stockholm Education Lnstitute.
INDE was able to resume its international contacts, begin computerization and prorvote skills
upgrading for its staff in the context of the SIDA projects, through supervision of mastors' Lheses
and the organization of studv visits to Sweden and Southern African countnes.
From 26-28 September 1995, with the support of the Dutch Embassy and SIDA, !NDE P rgani zed aF
Conference on Curriculum Development at which the Minister of Education announecd the rrfl
to INDE of the task of curriculum development, while it would also continue to carry out tho
necessarv research.
The Institute also regained its capacity to cooperate with United Nations agencies. it is lruning a
program of bilingual education for children supported by UND, it began to orgari7e stwly visits %r
its professional staff to a wvider range of countries; and it receives technical assistance thrrough
UNESCO.
At this stage. INDE will seek to make use of the experience generated in various projects that aTe
presently being implemented by the Ministry of Education with assistance from donoi-s. The
Ministry has developed a Strategic Plan for the Education Sector, covering 1 9982 002, whi"Th
brings together the various projects in the focus of increasing the access and improving the
quality of primary education. One of these projects is the OSUWELA project for pre- and inT)
service training of primarv school teachers, which is underway in Nampula provincee vviLl Fin.rIiu,g
from the Netherlands Embassy. Other innovative projects iliclude those funded by D-n.idca in
Tete Province, the teacher training courses by ADPP, the "Our School" project by 1TTINFF, as
well as the IAP (Institute for Improvement in Teaching) capacity building progra,n, sbyred. hy
