UNDP. IAP developed teaching material and strategies for in-service training for teachers, which
could be used as the basis for the rapid development of a curriculum for initial teacher training
B.    PROJECT JUSTIFICATION
1.     The problem
The mere widening of educational opportunity through rehabilitating the schools that were
destroyed during the war and building new ones would in no way guarantee the quality of education
needed for facing the challenges of consolidating peace and democracy, promoting development
and strengthening individual capacities.
At the same time, only an education that is attractive and relevant to these challenges will attract the
attention of families and permit more boys and girls to be retained as pupils, promoting the
improved efficiency of the educational system as a whole.
It is therefore necessarv to look at "curriculum transformation", understood as an integrated process
of promoting changes in the academic structures and the teaching and learning practices in the
schools, from the starting point of a new and relevant educational concept. This process should be
nurtured with adequate "course materials", such as Curriculum Designs, pupils' textbooks and
teachers' manuals: and should be sustained by constantly reviewed social and political agreements,
management capacity and relevant studies and research.
In Mozambique a 7-vear Integrated Basic Education must be introduced, oriented towards the
satisfaction of basic learning needs and overcoming both the extreme fragmentation between the
teaching programs for the specific subjects and the disparity between the EPI and EP2 institutional
models. These problems result in a sharp break between the organizational models with which the
boys and girls must interact and make it difficult to concentrate teachers in a single school,
exhausting them by the need to interact with an excessively high number of pupils.
It is therefore vital to prepare a guiding instrument for transforming the teaching-leaming processes
in the schools. i.e. an Integrated Curriculum Design which has conceptual unity for the whole
period of time that it is necessarv and possible to devote to guaranteeing basic education.
At the same time there must be appropriate training of the teachers currently in service. Bodies
coming under the Ministry of Education are working in this direction through various projects.
However. Mozambique does not vet have a Basic Curriculum Design that enables ratification of the
different existing programns for initial teacher training and in-service upgrading - which,
furthermore, is done separately for EP I and EP2 teachers.
The Basic Curriculum Design for Teacher Training throughout primary education should facilitate
the programming and implementation of different initial training and upgrading programs,
guaranteeing the presence of a core of knowledge and basic skills in all of them. It should also
facilitate a first guideline towards two major multi-disciplinary fields for the highest classes in
primary education.
Both the Integrated Curriculum Design for Basic Education and the Basic Curriculum Design for
Teacher Training should retrieve and develop new educational concepts that respond to the needs
and demands of different social actors and incorporate contributions from the different fields of
knowledge. In order to guarantee society's commitment and institutional continuity the essential
features of these concepts must be concretized in agreements of various kinds: general declarations,
and legal andlor administrative provisions.



Annex 1 1
Page 4 of 11
The pedagogical and didactic concepts that underlie the current plans, programs. textbooks and
teachers' manuals implied a break with the colonial educational concepts, and contain rudiments
that are relevant to an education adapted to the population's needs: for example. the inclusion of
references to the rural environment. the absence of references that discriminate against
Mozambicans. and a preliminary approach to the question of teaching Portuguese as a second
language.
But at the same time these concepts correspond to a traditional paradigm that is now being left
behind. The textbooks barely recognize the pupils' previous knowledge, and tend to promote
learning exclusively through repetition and memorizing.
The teachers' manuals provide them w ith a detailed day by day program. even in some cases hour
by hour. Their production represented a huge effort to provide teachers with little training a starting
point that would enable them to manage the school routines. But the way they were conceived did
not facilitate a reinforcement of the teachers' ability to understand the principles that would enable
them to achieve better results or adapt their teaching to the conditions of student groups in specific
communities.
The result is that the pupils' main classroom activity is "listen, wait and copy".
The production of new pupils' textbooks and guidelines for the work of the teachers is therefore
indispensable.
The availability of appropriate and intemallv consistent course materials is a necessary but
insufficient condition for a real curriculum transformation. The educational institutions must be
monitored as they adopt the new materials, through appropriate strategies for curriculum
management. In order to develop these strategies a curriculum management capacity must be
created. i.e. the capacity to program and promote the appropriate use of good new course materials
in varying conditions: schools small and large. rural and urban, with students from different
cultures. 7-year or not. materials better or worse communicated, with longer or shorter school days,
with or without experience in bilingual education, etc. This capacity should be created in a
deconcentrated fonm. and not only in the central bodies of the Ministry of Education.
Finally. challenges such as those proposed above require the incorporation of research results,
intemational comparisons. monitoring and evaluation. organized by a solid institution that has
adequate staff. infrastructure and resources. Only the development of research, monitoring and
evaluation capabilities in the field of teaching-leaming can facilitate a move from the logic of
educational refonns that are carried out at regular intervals to the logic of penmanent innovation that
enables gradual improvements to be introduced.
2.     Expected results at the end of the project
It is anticipated that by the end of the project there will be a series of inputs and adequate conditions
created for the permanent improvement of the quality of education in Mozambique.
(i)    There wvill be an Integrated Curriculum Design appropriate for the expansion of Basic
Education and the improvement of its quality and efficiency, reasonably validated.



Annex 11
Page 5 of 11
(ii)    There will be a Basic Curriculum Design for Training General Teachers for 7-year Primarv
Education, reasonably validated.
(iii)   There will be appropriate agreements guaranteeing the institutionalization and continuitv
of he process of curriculum transformation
(iv)    There will be a complete set of course materials for pupils and teachers in a 7-year Primary
Education program. consistent with the Integrated Curriculum Design for Basic Education,
reasonably validated.
(v)    There wvill be a team of technicians trained in curriculum management. extending to every
province and district.
(vi)   There w-ill be a collection of studies and research on curriculum development.
(vii) INDE wvill have been strengthened as an institution dedicated
to research. development and training in curriculum questions.
3.     Beneficiary groups
The main project beneficiaries are the pupils in Basic Education, who will be able to get a higher-
quality education focussed on satisfying their basic leaming needs, which is up to date and relevant
to their integration as citizens aind to improving their productive capacities. The people of
Mozambique in general will also benefit from the project. given that the education that will develop
from the new course materials. the birth of consensus about their fundamentals and the
improvement of curriculumn management and research capacity will contribute to improving
conditions for human. and therefore for economic, political and social development.
The project will pax special attention to the participation and performance of girls in the
education system by improving teacher training, designing curricula and materials that are
gender-sensitive. anud by promoting awareness of gender issues among educational managers.
The new curriculum is further expected to promote greater participation of parents, including
mothers. in the management of schools.
The project will address gender issues in the following manner:
a)  involvement of the Gender Units of the Provincial Directorates for Education in the
process of curriculum reform, with the specific task of ensuring that local specificity
wvith regard to gender are adequately taken care of.
b)  integration of female teachers and other female education staff in carrying out studies
on current problems with the curriculum, in formulating proposals for change and in
testing and monitoring new approaches.
c)  screening of all guidelines and documents produced for gender sensitivity.
d)  inclusion of gender specific components in the monitoring and evaluation activities of
the project.
4.     Project strategy
The curriculum strategies developed in past decades had five main characteristics. Firstly, they
resorted almost exclusively to the opinions of specialists in each subject field. Secondly, they
concentrated decision-taking at the higher levels of government. Thirdly, they omitted validation
processes prior to the large-scale adoption of the course materials. Fourthly, they omitted the



Annex 11
Page 6 of I I
training of intermediate curriculum management teams, and fifthlv,. thev omitted the creation of
conditions for transiting from a logic of periodic reforms to a logic of permanent curriculum
innovation through strengthening institutional capacity for curriculum innovation, research,
development and training.
This project proposes to overcome these strategic weaknesses through the following: i) considering
the various curriculum sources simultaneousIy ii) building consensus, iii) validation, evaluation
and monitoring: iv) training-participation-action: and v) capacity building.
4.1.   Considering five curriculum sources simultaneously
The process of preparing the course materials that will nurture the Curriculum Transfomiation of
Basic Education in Mozambique. including that of Teacher Training for Primary Education, will be
supported from five sources.
The first source comprises the needs and demands of education as formulated by representatives of
national social and political organizations, enriched by proposals set out in intemational documents
signed by the Govern-rment of Mozamnbique.
The second source is different kinds of proposals for subject content (conceptual. procedural,
attitudinal) that can be tauglht according to the Mozambican academic communitv.
TIhe third source comprises the results of curriculum research carried out in Mozambique and the
results of the evaluations and monitoring studies that will take place as part of the project.
The fourth source is made up of successful intemational experiences of curriculum transformation
and their products. whdich can be studied on the ground with the help of study visits or through
publications and the contributions of intemational consultants and experts.
The fifth source will be "good teaching experience". using experienced teachers to "filter" the
subject contents from the perspective of whether they contribute to different basic leaming needs
and their relevance to the national educational realitv, selecting and organizing those that are richest
pedagogically and developing them as necessary in order to guarantee their teaching and leaming.
4.2.   Building consensus
Considering the needs and demands of education as one of the sources for building the curriculum
encourages the possibility that the curriculum will be perceived as belonging to society as a whole,
but does not guarantee it.
'The needs and demands of education can be identified in different ways. One is to carry out studies
and research oriented to this end. This brings with it the risk that, while the needs detected and the
proposals for satisfying them have been detenmined in an appropriate way, the curriculum proposals
as a whole come to be perceived as the exclusive patrimony of a small group of researchers and
writers from the State or from a government that is necessarily transitory.
To minimize this risk a strategy of building consensus will be used. This will consist in creating a
National Advisorv Commission on Curriculum Transformation, with a membership based on
criteria of political, economic, social, religious, academic and cultural representation.



Annex 11
Page 7 of 11
The Commnission's functions will be to channel society's demands in relation to Basic Education and
build consensus around the essential features of the curriculum transformation, with the purpose of
guaranteeing continuity in the context of a multi-party regime.
The Commission will have about 50 members, with the following tentative composition:
Members of MINED
Members of the Assembly's Education Commission
Rectors of the universities
Representatives of the main religious faiths
Social and political personalities from the provinces (2 per province)
Representatives of the main Mozambican NGOs
Representatives of the main business and trade union associations
The Advisory Commission will meet regularly, analyze the various proposals prepared by INDE
and channeled to it througlh the Ministry of Education, propose ways of formalizing the consensus
achieved through agreements or legal or administrative norms, and will draft these documents,
taking them to other relevant bodies as appropriate.
4.3.   Validation, evaluation and monitoring
Even though experienced teachers participate in the production of the various course materials,
there is always the risk that the product will be remote from the reality of the schools, the language
of the children and the possibilities for creative use by the teachers. To avoid this risk there will be
a strategy of preparing separate versions of the materials, which will be validated and modified on
the basis of their use and of evaluations of processes and learning achievements and monitoring
graduates, in accordance witlh the tvpe of material.
A first complete Draft of the Integrated Curriculum Design for Primary Education will be validated
through use in nonnal teaching conditions in a designed sample of 22 7-year schools located in
different provinces and witlh distinct characteristics: urban, rural, large, small, serving defined
linguistic communities. etc. The results of the use of this Draft will enable it to be transformed into
a First Edition of the Integrated Curriculum Design.
The design and implementation of a first sanmpling operation for evaluating processes and leaming
achievements in a representative sample of 7-year primary schools will provide knowledge of the
starting point prior to generalizing the use of the new course materials. A second sampling will
enable evaluation of the first stage of generalization of the First Edition of the Integrated
Curriculum Design for Primary Education and of the teaching materials. The data gathered from
this and the contributions from various basic research projects will enable the production of a
Second Edition of the Design and the materials.
Given the degree of urgencv in changing teacher training, the fact that there are only 18 Teacher
Training Centers in the country and the need to evaluate the teacher training through its impact, it is
proposed to prepare a Draft of the Basic Curriculum Design to be used directly in all the training
establishments, do a monitoring study of the pedagogical techniques of the first graduates and make
an immediate revision of the Draft, transforming it into a First Edition of the Basic Curriculum
Design.



Annex I1
Page 8 of 11
4.4. Training-participation-action
With the aim of ensuring greater participation from the educational actors themselves in the
preparation of course materials, and at the same time of guaranteeing upgrading skills for the
deconcentrated management of curriculum transformation. a process of training-participation-action
will be organized-
A National Curriculum Management Team will be established, with the following functions: i)
training itself for the increasingly decentralized management of curriculum transforinationw ii)
participating in the preparation of course materials: iii) participating in designing a strategy for
transforming the Teacher Training Centers: and iv) participating in designing an increasingly
decentralized strategy for generalizing the Curriculum Transfonnation of Primary Education.
The National Curriculum Management Team will have approximately 350 members as followvs:
I    Heads of Curriculum Transformation (1 per province)
4 1    Ministrv of Education Inspectors
1 20    Pedagogical Technicians presently in the Ministry of Education (I per Department)
22     Directors of the validating primary schools
I 8    Directors of the Teacher Training Institutes
110    Experienced primary school directors (approximately 10 per province)
A program of training-participationi-action w-ill be planned for developing with this team in three
phases: i) conceptual strengtlheninig and participation in preparing the Draft and the First Version of
the Integrated Curriculum Design for Primary Education. ii) Design and preparation of a strategy
for monitoring the generalization of the Integrated Curriculum Design for Basic Education with
support from the ZIPs. and iii) Monitoring the process of generalizing the Integrated Curriculum
Design with support from the ZIPs.
It is anticipated that the training-participation-action program will guarantee 320 hours of individual
activitv. divided into two meetings per year from month 7 of the project onwards, with phases of
work on the ground in between meetings. The provision of approximately 4,000 pages of reading
material per team participant is anticipated. at a rate of roughly 1,000 pages per year.
4.5. Capacity building
It is planned to continue with capacity building for curriculum development, bringing in consultants
from a broader range of countries. developing a program of study visits for looking at educational
research and curriculum transformation programs in other countries, and promoting participation in
intemational meetings on these questions.
It is also planned to organize dialogues and debates at international level in Mozambique, with the
presence of professionals in policy design and curriculum research and development, aimed at
opening up more possibilities for exposure to current concepts and experiences and nurturing the
training-participation-action processes. taking advantage of the visitors as speakers or panelists in
the planned activities and also as skilled contacts for the Advisory; Commission.
The continuation and updating of the process of equipping and computerizing INDE is also
planned, with the aim of enabling it to supply the needs of the project, particularly through the rapid
reproduction of small quantities of the printed materials, making maximum use of new technologies
for producing them and for providing access to and processing of national and intemational
information.



Annex 11
Page 9 of 11
Reinforcing INDE's librarv as a specialized library in Curriculum Research and Development is
also planned, providing it with the academic material, textbooks and teachers' manuals it needs for
its activities.
4.6    A process approach
The present project document presents a general outline of the strategies and activities of the
project during a period of five years and also a specific activitv plan. The outline is still rather
general in nature, the reason for this being that it will onlv be possible to fill in the contours of the
curriculum plan and how it will be designed and tested once existing research on problems in the
curriculum has been svstematized. further studies have been conducted and the consultation
process on what basic education should entail has been carried out. A process approach is
therefore required in this project whereby subsequent steps in the project are defined and taken as
a clearer picture emerges of learning needs and outcomes, how the curriculum reform will take
place and what its implications will be. This process approach is based on the assumptions that:
a) An exercise of tlis nature requires careful reflection and broad-based consultation to
ensure that needs are adequately identified and different dimensions addressed. A
process-approach is therefore required bv which the results of past evaluations and
discussions w itlh different stakelholders are translated into strategies and proposals which
are tested, validated or rejected and then implemented on a wider scale. In this manner,
the Ministry of Education hopes to ensure that a framework and conditions are
established which will ensure that curriculum reform becomes an integral part of the
education process and an on-going activity.
b) Specific activities and strategies will be decided upon once a more concrete proposal for
the curriculum has been prepared. This proposal will be prepared during the inception
phase of the project and will be based on a process of extensive research and consultation
whilst at the sane time activities are implemented which will ensure that the necessary
conditions exist to experiment and launch the Integrated Plan. The inception phase
(covering the first two y ears) w1ill therefore focus, amongst others, on:
*  svstematization of existing studies;
*  carrying out further studies in areas where more information is required (two
such studies are presently on-going with funds which the Netherlands Embassy
has advanced to INDE).
*  designing and establishing an organizational structure for the project including
the establishment of the Steering Committee (which will integrate representatives
from the Ministry of Education, UNESCO and the various donors), the National
Consultative Forum for Curriculum Transformation, and the National and
Provincial Groups for the Promotion of Curriculum Transformation.
*  on-the-job training for staff at INDE and at provincial education directorates;
*  the definition and discussion of leaming outcomes for students leaving grades 5
and 7:
*  setting-up of Resource Centres for the Promotion of Curriculum Transformation
in all of the I I Provincial Education Directorates.
*  broad-based discussions with stakeholders from various levels and various walks
of life. including extensive consultations with the ZIPs on the proposed
curriculum.
The outcome of the inception phase will be an Integrated Plan for the Curriculum for Basic
Education and Teacher Training which will be based on broad-based discussions and
consultations with various stakeholders in the education process, on the results of studies and



Annex I I
Page 10 of 11
evaluations of the current education system, and on comparisons with examples of good practice
from other areas of the world. This plan includes a curriculum framework with outcomes,
learning areas, categories of content, didactical approaches, types of materials and monitoring
procedures and will also provide summarized syllabi for each subject area. A summary of the
expected outputs for the inception phase can be found attached to this Activity Appraisal
Document.
This Plan will guide the activities of the project over the remaining three y ears during which the
following broad areas of activity will take place:
* extensive testing of guidelines, materials and approaches in 22 schools from different
areas of the country and revision of the relevant aspects on the basis of the results;
* in-service training of education staff at different level to introduce. monitor and provide
support to the process of curriculum transfornation;
* gradual introduction of the new curriculum;
* establishment of an organizational framework and institutional capacity capable of
promoting a process of continuous curricular experimentation which becomes
increasingly decentralized.
Such a process-approach will require careful monitoring and sufficient flexibility to allow
strategies and plans to be changed (quite dramatically, if necessary) on the basis of results to be
obtained. These changes mav even affect the duration of the project, its staffing, budgets, etc.
To allow for flexibility key dimensions of the present project are, therefore:
a) Division of the project into two phases. The inception phase (outlined above as well as in
the attached project document) which will have a duration of two years, followed by an
implementation phase (with a probable duration of 3 years) covering testing, adaptation
and gradual introduction of the new curriculum.
b) Two independent evaluations, the first of which will take place in months 22-24 of the
project. that is at the end of the inception phase. This evaluation will determine project
activities from vear three onwards. The second independent evaluation will take place at
the end of the five y ear period.
c) Establishment of a Steering Committee to monitor the progress of the project against
plans. to approve activitv and financial plans for the following period, and to formnulate
recommendations on changes in approaches, activities, etc. The mandate of this Steering
Committee is outlined in more detail under paragraph G below.
d) Establishment of various other consultative bodies and working teams at different levels
to ensure that decisions are based careful consideration of policies, priorities and realities
of the education system.
5.     Institutional agreements
The project will be carried out bv the Government of Mozambique and located in INDE. It will
rely on assistance from UNESCO for administration, coordination of international technical
assistance, guidance for the organization of study visits abroad, and permanent technical assistance
throughout its activities. A team of three people will guarantee UNESCO's participation in the
project: a Chief Technical Adviser, an Administrative and Secretarial Assistant and a driver.
6.     Special considerations
Curriculum transformation projects are particularly sensitive to influences of ideas and the transfer
of developments from other realities. When these ideas and developments do not correspond to a
representative range of the state of current thinking at international level, the process and its



Annex 11
Page 11 of 11
products risk being impoverished. On the other hand, when contributions from different countries
and institutional frameworks are added together in an uncoordinated way, the danger is that they
will not be used to best advantage. Special attention will therefore be given to promoting contact
with a varied range of ideas and experiences and coordinating the contributions.
At the same time, curriculum transfornation in reality begins in the respective directorates. This
project will therefore also support two National Directorates, which will be responsible for
generalizing the use of the new materials and creating a new educational culture in the schools.
Coordination with the Directorate of Primary Education and the body in charge of Teacher Training
will therefore be a priority throughout the project.
Finally, this project does not aim to produce all the basic and applied research needed to make an
adequate response to the challenges of curriculum development, but only that considered to be an
absolute priority. INDE is also undertaking projects for a survey of spoken Portuguese in
Mozambique and the experimental development of bilingual education, the continuity of which do
not form part of this project. Both projects may provide relevant data for decision-taking on
curricula, and their continuity would enable a significant advance in knowledge about some critical
questions.
7.     Coordination
A Project Steering Committee will be formed with the function of monitoring and supervising the
project. The Committee will have a tripartite representation, with participation from the
Govemment (Ministry of Education/INDE), the donor countries, and UNESCO. This
Committee will meet every six months to analyse the implementation of project activities and
fonnulate recommendations for the following stage. Extraordinary meetings may be convened
when necessary. The UNESCO office in Maputo will be responsible for the organisation the
progress meetings in collaboration with INDE. The meetings will be chaired by the Ministry of
Education. Decisions of the Steering Committee will be taken by consensus.
The Steering Committee will have the following tasks:
- to approve the annual operational and financial plans
- to appraise the six-monthly progress report, submitted by the executing agency
- to decide on all proposed major changes in project approach, activities, staffing and budget
- to ensure that all agreements under the project are complied with, and the that project is
implemented according to the project document or to any subsequent amendments agreed
upon by the Steering Committee
- to approve the Terms of Reference of all technical reviews and independent evaluations of
the project and decide on follow-up, if required, to the conclusions and recommendations
which arise from these reviews and/or evaluations
Minutes of the meetings of the Steering Committee will be prepared by UNESCO in
collaboration with INDE. They will be distributed within two weeks after the meeting.

MOZAMBIQUE
Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP)
Institutional Development
Introduction
A systematic approach to the institutional development of MINED was initiated in 1995, with
assistance from various donors, to strengthen the management capacity of the Ministry especially
in a number of critical areas. These initiatives were important following the war, which disrupted
activities in most parts of the country, and given the generally limited number of qualified people
in the country. This effort has been supported bv Bank operations in Mozambique. especially the
Capacity Building Project. which is supporting secondary and higher education to provide a pool
of qualified people to manage the civil service and the private sector. This is especially important
for MINED which needs to manage an expanding program in a rapidly growing economy, and
which is also gradually decentralizing management of the sector to the provinces and districts to
improve efficiency in the deliver! of services. The policies and measures introduced over this
period were based on a global assessment of current management practices and capability,
including operational procedures and methods. This assessment, as well as the development and
implementation of the program, were conducted using a participatory approach. This has resulted
in ownership and an identification with the objectives and activities by staff at all levels in
MINED. The program was also designed using a modular approach to permit flexibility in the
introduction of revisions as the needs of MINED evolve. MINED plans to build on this
foundation by conducting a more thorough analysis of each administrative unit of the Ministry at
the central, provincial and district levels. as a basis for defining the capacity needed in each
section and at each level for efficient and quality delivery of education. This analysis will be
completed prior to Programn Effectiveness, and implementation of the recommendations will be
integrated witlh the other measures and investments planned under the ESSP.
As currently defined the capacity building program will cover:
improvement of the organizational structure and management systems of the ministry;
improvement of planning and monitoring of programs;
development of the capacity for managing and coordinating programs;
strengthening of financial and procurement management
improvement of the management of human resources; and
development of a public relations capacity in the ministry.
Within these broad areas, special attention will be paid to areas critical to the education sector
such as training of head-teachers. capacitv needed for upgrading the Zonas de Influencia
Pedagogica (ZIP). training of inspectors and training of provincial and district level staff.
Current Status of Implementation
Implementation of the program was initiated in 1996 with a seminar for the top managers in
MINED, namely the Minister, the Vice-Minister and the Permanent Secretary. The purpose of
the seminar was to discuss the mission of MINED, assess the capacity of the structure to carry out
its mission, and more specifically to examine the role of the leadership of MINED, and to
determine how the leadership could be strengthened to perform its role more effectively. This
seminar was followed by a seminar for all managers and key staff at the central level and
Provincial Education Directors in Matola in August 1998, and a management seminar for the
province of Nampula in September 1998. The annual co-ordination seminar with the provinces



Annex 12
Page 2 of 4
and districts, at which an evaluation of the annual Nvork program is discussed. and plans made for
the following year, was expanded to include key staff from all levels and used as an opportunitv
to discuss the mission of MINED and management procedures and behavior at all levels. These
seminars will be an integral part of the capacity building process. Bv March 1999. each Province
would have organized a seminar based on the e-,perience at Nampula and focused on local
planning and implementation capability for the ESSP. Some partners already expressed their
interest to assist MINED in undert.aking this task.
Key Issues and Actions Taken
Coordination of Implementation. An important decision of MINED vxas not to carrv-out a radical
restructuring of the Ministry. How-ever. the need for strong coordination of the implementation of
the ESSP was fully recognized- and a decision was made to set-up an implementation structure
within which this would be done. This was urgenit given the decision not to use a separate
Program Implementationi Unit (PIU) to implement the Progranm. A Comissao Instaladora
(Installation Committee) chaired bv the Vice-Minister was therefore established to recommend an
appropriate framework for implementation of the ESSP. The basic structure (Attachment l) and
termns of reference for the ESS P implementation. as proposed by the Installationi Conmmittee, are
described below. The overall structure for implementation would be as follows:
i)     The Ministcr of Education will continue to chair the Consultative Council (CC)
of MINED. which is responisible for policy fonrulation and coordination within and outside the
ministrv, until the ncw structure is ful]y functional and the CC mission is reviewed.
ii)    A SteCrin,g Com}lmiette (Comite Paritario de Acompanhamiiento, COPA) wvill be
the main vehicle for ensurling donor coordination and for monitoring and evaluating
implementation progress of the ESSP. The committee is composed of the Minister of Education.
the Vice-Minister, eiglht otlher MINED officials. one from the Ministry of Planning and Finance,
one from the Ministrv of State Administration. and nine representatives of the extemal partners in
the education sector. The 2 1 -member COPA is scheduled to meet three times per year.
Observers from MINED and partniers wiII be allowed to participate in COPA meetings on a case-
by-case basis.
iii)    A Technical Committee (TC) of the ministry will deal with technical issues. The
Vice-Minister will head the TC. which will be composed of national directors, the chairs of the
working groups. and individuals nominlated b the Minister of Education based on their expected
contributions to the implemiientation of the program. The program Executive Secretarv
(Secretario Executivo) will also be a member of the TC. No donors will be represented in this
committee.
iv)    Working Groups (U Gs) -viii be responsible for dealing with technical issues
related to the various componenits, including the preparation of technical papers, annual work
plans. annual review- of implementation. and advice on changes in the design or policies related to
the various components. These WGs will be horizontal in their composition and will be headed
by the appropriate Director or his/ler designee. The WGs will only exist for the duration
required to solve or implement a component. However, some WGs, such as the WG on Finance
and Procedures. will be permanent. This organization follows existing structures put in place by
MINED and used during the preparation of the program. The Installation Committee agreed
upon seven working groups: education quality- access and gender; institutional development;
voc/tec; adult education: higher education and research; financial management and procedures.
The overall organization has been approved by the Steering Committee (October 22, 1998) and
the terms of reference for the Steering Committee, the Technical Council, the Executive



Annex 12
Page 3 of 4
Secretariat, Institutional Development WG, and Financial and Procedures WG have been
completed.
v)     A Secretariat, composed of an Executive Secretary (Secretario Executivo) and a
few support staff, will provide administrative services to COPA, TC. and WG in coordinating and
monitoring implementation of the program, including regular donor coordination.
Within this frarnework, national directors and heads of autonomous institutions xvill take
responsibility for preparation and implementation of the program. A formal decree (No. 26/98)
establishing this structure was approved in August 1998. A similar implementation organization
will be set up at the provincial level.
Staff Qualitv in MINED. One of the challenges facing MINED is the low quality of staff over the
years and the inability to attract and retain good staff. Until rccentlv. qualified staff have been
leaving the ministry in large numbers for jobs in the university. and parastatals. In spite of this.
progress has been made in improving the quality of staff more recently. Most senior managers
now have graduate degrees. and appointment of graduates to head and staff provincial and district
directorates of education is progressing wN ell. Special attention is being paid to the less developed
provinces. An additional sixteen newv graduates in Economics, Finance and Accounting has
recently been posted to further strengthen offices in the provinces. especially in resource
management. The group of new graduates benefited from a short training course in planning.
financial, accounting and reporting before assuming their new responsibilities.
This progress is the result of a decision of MINED to improve staff quality. and a combination of
positive circumstances. The pool of qualified candidates in the education sector. including
teachers is increasing, and there is a Nwillingness to draw key managers from this pool. The good
performance of the economy has led to rising standards of living, low inflation and price
stabilization. Incentives of staff are also being improved through housing facilities, medical
insurance, and better pension conditions. Prospects for staff in MINED would be even better
with the plalned civil service reform which would permnit top managers to be offered special
conditions of employment and the decomiipression of salaries for all staff.
Future Activities
To further build on this foundation. MINED will carrv-out a more detailed institutional
assessment focusing on financial and procurement management, school construction.
management of ZIPs, district and provincial offices. An assessment of the financial and
procurement management has beeni made wvith recommendations on how it can be improved (see
Annexes 5 and 6). On the basis of this assessment, training programs have been scheduled in
these areas for staff at the central. provincial and districts prior to Program Effectiveness. The
program for this training will be based on the detailed findings of the planned detailed
assessment. A similar exercise has been undertaken for school construction with communities
and a preliminary assessment of capacity in this area has been done (see Annex 13). Proposals
for capacity building for the ZIPs has also been addressed in the context of plans for re-vitalizing
these units and are contained in the following documents:



Annex 12
Page 4 of 4
(a) Supervisao e ZIP
(b) Guia do Coordenador de ZIP
(c) Potencialidade da ZIP na Gestao de Formacao Continua de Professores
(d) Plano Nacional de Intervencao nas ZIP
The institutional assessment would build on previous activities and will be implemented in
collaboration with other donors active in the sector. It will be aimed at determining whether the
units of MINED, and all other agencies/institutions involved in the implementation of the ESSP.
have core capacity to implement the proposed program and, if necessary. to introduce measures to
strengthen this capacity. This would also include determining what needs to be done to improve
the long-term capacity of the Ministry to deliver services, beyond the implementation of this
program.
The methodology to be used in performing this institutional assessment is based on an operational
definition of capacity. Applying this definition, the capacity of all the organizations involved in
the implementation of the proposed programr will be analyzed by focusing on organization,
leadership, financial resources, material resources, human resources and work practices. The
assessment teamn will focus first on the question 'what needs to be done in order to implement the
program?', or 'for what is capacity required'?'. It will then determine 'who needs to do it'?'. or
'whose capacity within the education sector is in question'?' Answering these two questions. the
team will systematically identify all the organizations, and the organizational units, which are to
be involved in the implementation of the proposed investment program. Once these are
identified, the team will examine the five factors indicated above with respect to each and every
organization and organizational unit individually. This examination will help determine
institutional weaknesses and capacity gaps.
Four types of altemative capacity building measures will then be considered in each case where a
capacity gap is identified:
*  To change practices, ways of doing things and procedures, where such a change can improve
the capacity of the organization or unit involved. without having to allocate it more resources.
*  To allocate, or reallocate, financial and human resources for the performance of certain
activities, where the gaps are considered to be the result of inadequate resources.
*  To spread certain activities over a longer period of time, or to reduce the scope of certain
development objectives, where additional capacity is required but cannot be built
immediately, or during the currently scheduled implementation period.
* To follow a certain mix of these three alternatives.
The institutional assessment team will consist of two MOE officials and the Education Advisor,
supported by a Bank Team. The officials and the consultant will be trained to apply the
methodology. By the end of this assessment, they will be able to use the methodology to carry
out similar assessments independently. The managers of each organization and unit assessed will
be thoroughly briefed on the objectives of the capacity building initiative, as well as the
methodology, and will take an active part in the assessment and identification of solutions.
By the end of the assessment exercise, the team will produce a matrix showing unit-by-unit the
capacity gaps identified and the capacity building measures proposed to address these gaps.
Attachment II is a sample of the matrix based on the financial management requirements. If
necessary, implementation of some components would be rescheduled to meet the requirements
for capacity building. All agreed actions will be included in the program's implementation plan.
A review of capacity will be conducted annually as part of the implementation review with
donors.

MOZAMBIQUE
Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP)
SCHOOL CONSTRUCTION
PART 1- PRIMARY SCHOOL CONSTRUCTION
Background
Within the framework of the Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP), Mozambique aims to expand its
primary school network by 12,000 classrooms in the next 5 years, an increase over existing stock of about
62%. To meet this expansion all organizations and entities that have traditionally participated in the
construction of classrooms, i.e. the communities, the NGOs, donors and the state, need to play a role. How
to monitor, stimulate, coordinate and,_possibly, support the efforts of all these role-players is one of the
tasks MINED, through its proposed Working Group on Access and Gender, needs to address. Thus, the
attainment of the expansion targets set by ESSP will depend to a large extent on efforts outside the direct
control of the ministry and therefore requires close supervision and collaboration between communities and
the District or Provincial Education Offices. During the past five years, about 5,000 classrooms were
constructed, NGOs and communities contributed about 30% and 55% respectively, leaving for MINED a
share of 15%, almost entirely financed from IDA credits. Because construction by NGOs and communities
during this period was closely linked to the Peace Accord, the repatriation and resettlement and the ready
availability of relief funds (inter alia UNHCR), it is not clear what the future contribution of these groups
will be except for the CSP program. NGO activity has certainly declined in recent years with fewer
sources of funds available. No data are available on community effort.
For the purpose of quantifying and costing primary school construction of ESSP, the Governmnent has
proposed a scenario whereby MINED will contribute about 1/3 of future classroom output, NGOs will
continue to contribute less than 1/3 whilst the community share will be above 1/3'. By applying this
distribution scenario to future construction targets in each province, the number of classrooms to be built
by each group has been calculated. Construction targets are based on priorities defined according to i)
projections of student population growth, ii) accessibility of sites, iii) current construction activity and iv)
population density. In the proposed scenario, 87% of the classrooms would be in rural areas and provinces
with highest priority would be Cabo Delgado, Nampula and Niassa. During the appraisal mission in
October it was agreed that the final selection of sites would be based on actual enrollment ratios, in the
regions and the results of the school mapping exercise.
Primary school construction by MINED will increasingly involve i) the participation of communities in the
planning and implementation of projects, ii) more sustainable construction technology and iii) decentralized
management of the delivery process. To this end MINED is in the course of developing a Community
School Program (CSP) which will gradually become responsible for the construction and rehabilitation of
all primary schools. Whilst the pivotal role of the comnmunity in the delivery process remains a constant
factor, actual construction under this program might be carried out i) by communities themselves or ii)
subcontracted, in part or entirely, to NGOs, labour brigades or contractors. - One of the spin-offs of the
progran is the development of contracting capacity at the local, district and village levels while injecting
additional resources into the local economy.
' Refer GEPE-MINED, ESSP Civil Works Component, April 1998



Annex 13
Page 2 of 9
In order to ensure that sufficient classrooms are built during the early stages of the CSP, and in those areas
where communities are unwilling to play their role in the delivery process, the goverinent might continue
to build new primary schools using the traditional channels of procurement, including National Competitive
Bidding and the use of medium size and large contractors,
COMMUNITY SCHOOL PROGRAM
The Community School Program (CSP) is a national program aimed at improving and enlarging the
country's stock of primary schools with maximum participation and support of the target community. In
addition, the program is seen as a vehicle for accelerating community ownership, governance and
responsibility for its educational institutions. School construction would not be constrained by its
traditional categorization as "civil works" but would form an integrated part of the strategy towards quality
education in Mozambique.
Basic principles
The basic principle underlying the design of the program is to maintain and strengthen the ability and
capacity of communities to develop their own schools and improve the functionality and lifespan of the
structures.
Features of the program are: i) the central role of the community in the delivery and management of
schools, ii) the provision by government of financial and technical support to commnunities, iii) the
utilization of sustainable and affordable building technology, iv) financial or material contribution by the
community to the construction and maintenance of the school, v) the planning and coordinating function of
the provincial and district education authorities, vi) the involvement of local building brigades, local
contractors, NGOs and other agencies in the implementation of the program and vii) maximum
developmental impact of the program through improving skills, local job opportunities and
entrepreneurship.
The Community School Program (CSP) will assist communities with the financial means and the technical
assistance to i) add new classrooms to existing schools, ii) replace existing non-functional classrooms or iii)
build new schools in new locations. In addition, CSP will assist communities with improving works which
have already been started or completed.
CSP will work very closely with the provincial education authorities (DPE) who will appoint a project
coordinator in each province. The local commnunity, represented by the School Councils as well as the local
civil authorities, will be involved in the location and planning of the school and, depending on the
circumstances, manage the construction themselves or use local contractors or NGOs for the execution of
the work. Technical supervision, procurement assistance and building support will be provided by
MINED/GEPE through its provincial representatives in collaboration with DPE technicians, local
consultants and NGOs. To kick start the program and ensure larger coverage, NGOs will also participate
either by i) managing construction on behalf of the community, ii) supervising construction and iii) training
artisans and supervisors.



Annex 13
Page 3 of 9
Classroom Design
The aim is to create a functional learning and teaching environment by means of a structure that is easy to
build and adaptable to take into account regional differences in building tradition, climate, materials and
skills. Building specifications will also aim to maximize on site production of materials and components
and enhance labour content of the buildings. In high-density pern-urban areas schools will be adapted to
meet urban requirements for security, water, electricity supply and sewage disposal.
The design of the classroom will be based on successful yet cost effective models used in Mozambique and
in the region. Examples are the model developed by the Mozambique Program of the Lutheran World
Federation (LWF), the PRONES (Para Nossa Escola) classroom developed by UNICEF as well as the
classroom model used in Zambia by the Social Recovery Project (SRP). All these models are extensively
documented (with manuals, materials lists etc) and involve an important community participation
component. All of the models meet the requirement of being functional and simple to build, but only the
LWF model has pre-designed adaptations to different building technologies and materials. Other models
might be available in Mozambique but would need to be explored.
At the start of the project MINED will analyze various models and prepare a range of suitable designs and
components. A number of pilot classrooms and schools will be built to test different design aspects,
provide training opportunities for construction supervisors and illustrate the range of options to officials
and the public. The cost estimate for these preparatory activities is provided in Attachment 4.
UNESCO is currently carrying out a study of existing classroom models, construction practices and costs
and will make recommendations for suitable standards and models for low-cost construction in
Mozambique. Once available, these recommendations will be taken into account in the CSP model.
CSP School
For the initial phase of the program the classroom design based on the LWF model will be used. This
model is closely resembling the PRONES and SRP models. The basic building unit is a 2-classroom block
with an office and storeroom between the classrooms. The advantages of the relatively short 2-classroom
block over longer three- and four-classroom blocks are i) the easy adaptation to the terrain, ii) better scale
within the village setting iii) reduction of noise transmission between classrooms and iv) easier to construct.
Plan variations comprising a 2 classroom block without office space as well as a 3-classroom block are
also available. The blocks can be arranged in clusters of two, three or more to make up the desired number
of classrooms for the school.
Design Data
The size of the proposed classroom is 55m2 for an optimum number of 48-50 pupils (I. 15m2/student). The
desk is 1200mm wide which allows, if necessary, 3 pupils per desk, expanding the seating capacity of the
classroom. VIP latrines will be provided on the basis of two for each classroom and each school will have
a water supply and one staff house for the school director



Annex 13
Page 4 of 9
Structural variations
The proposed model has four basic structural variations as well as additional variations based on climate
and soil conditions. In principle, all variations can be built by the community or by a contractor. The four
structural variations are:
i)   Timber structure comprising treated eucalyptus or indigenous timber poles with infill walls in soil-
cement.
ii)   Loadbearing soil-cement walls and piers
iii)  Concrete block structure and
iv)   Burnt clay brick structure
These variations correspond either to existing building traditions and available materials or potentlally
useful methods of construction. Bumt clay bricks are being used in the North and West of the country
(Niassa, Tete), concrete blocks are used in most other areas. Suitable timber poles are found in Manica and
Sofala and allow for rapid erection of structures. Soil-cement is not widely used in Mozambique but is
potentially the most practical and least expensive method of construction. Soil-ccment blocks are easily
made with hand-operated presses from material available on site and their manufacture requires little
special skills. More widespread application of soil-cement technology as a substitute for traditional "pau c
pique" would greatly improve the durability of comrnmunity built structures.
To minimize transport and labour cost, regional variations in construction method and materials would be
fully explored and allowed for in the adopted solutions. Generally the local method will be the least
expensive. However regional cost differences are unavoidable. Unfavorable swampy soil conditions like
those found in large parts of Sofala and Zambesia may require different, more expensive foundations.
Structural variations dependent on local conditions may lead to cost variations of up to US$2,000 per
classroom.
Climate control
In most parts of Mozambique some form of open structure is adequate and enclosure of classroom space
can be limited to security grilles and mosquito gauze. In certain parts of the country some formn of full
enclosure might be necessary. This can be done with glazed windows, louvres or shutters. Full enclosure
is expensive and will add about US$2,000 to the cost of a classroom. For instance, based on the LWF
model, adding shutters to a timber post and beam classroom increases the cost from US$7,903 to
US$10,532.
Labour enhanced construction
Enhancing the labour content of structures will reduce the cost of construction, generate local income,
enhance ownership and facilitate maintenance and expansion. NGOs routinely ask communities to produce
bricks or blocks as a community contribution. The CSP project will make equipment available to the
community to collect gravel and sand, produce concrete and/or soil-cement blocks and, where possible,
concrete or fibre-cement rooftiles. Equipment to saw rooftimbers or produce other timber components may
also be considered. On completion of the initial phase of the project, the equipment becomes the property
of the community who may use it for further expansion of the school and for income-generating activities.



Annex 13
Page 5 of 9
Director's house
The CSP program includes a 2-bedroom house, with living room, cooking porch and terrace, suitable as
Director's house. During appraisal it was agreed that staff accommodation provided by the project at each
primary school would be limited to one Director's house. Additional staff houses would be provided by
communities themselves.
Cost of construction
The base cost of a typical CSP 2-classroom block of 139m2 including an office and storeroom is estimated
at US$19,000 or around US$9,500 per classroom. Approximately 10% of the base cost is the value of
community labor, i.e. unskilled labor inputs like the collection of sand, gravel and water, the leveling of the
site and the making of blocks. By taking advantage of local materials and altemative construction
techniques, savings of US$1,000 per classroom might be obtained in certain areas. These savings will at
times be offset by additional transport or foundation costs in remote areas or in areas with difficult
foundation condition. Detailed cost information from the provinces will be available to reflect regional cost
differences once the school mapping exercise has been completed. To reflect the variance in cost, a blanket
location factor of 5% has been allowed. Assuming a small rural school comprising 4 classrooms, 2 offices,
sanitary facilities, water supply and one director's house, the cost of establishing one primary classroom
including fumiture, supervision and physical contingency is US$21,473 (Attachment 1, Table 1.2). The
equivalent cost of the current rural EPR program under IDA-Education II and the FINNIDA financed
PASE project is US$25,800* and US$43,950* respectively (*costs adjusted for reduction in staff houses).
Community Participation
Community participation does not mean that the community physically builds the school. Building work in
almost all societies is a specialized job requiring skill and experience and even in a rural "subsistence"
setting people will ask the "community" specialist to do the job. Community participation, within the CSP
framework, means that communities assume responsibility for the construction, the maintenance and the
management of the school. Under the proposed program communities will become the "developers" of the
school, while MINED and other donors become "Financiers" who provide funds to the community and
ensure that the schools are built according to an agreed design and standard.
The tasks of the community are to: i) apply to participate in the project and agree to conditions; ii) approve
the location, the scope of works, the design and the budget; iii) agree on the method of procurement, i.e.
direct labour, brigade or contractor; iv) organize community contributions in labour or cash; v) authorize
payments to contractors and suppliers; vi) take over the school on completion; and vii) maintain the
buildings in good condition.
The locus of community responsibility will be the School Council (Conselho Escolar) or LEC (Ligacao
Escola Communidade). The program will assist, where necessary with the formation of these bodies and
provide training and support before and during the construction phase. Where communities are weak or
not yet organized, the Director of the ZIP within whose area the construction is proposed, will be the
community representative. The District Administrator and the Chefe de Posto, who often represent the real
power and authority in the area will also be involved,



Annex 13
Page 6 of 9
In addition to its role as "developer" communities would also contribute financially or with labour to the
construction of the school. Communities would clear and level the site, collect sand, gravel and water and
make blocks. The value of these contributions would amount up to 10% of the construction cost. Within
the framework of CSP local modifications to the proposed models may be negotiated providingfor more
accommodation within a given budget.
PROJECT MANAGEMENT AND SUPERVISION
Direct responsibility for the management and supervision would be with the Provincial Directors of
Education (DPE) through a contracted project coordinator (Architect/ Engineer) currently seconded to the
province by MINED/GEPE. The coordinator would be assisted by a deputy who would be full-time on
CSP. Non-technical provincial and district staff would also be involved in aspects of planning and
community mobilization.
Technical supervision
The success of CSP will depend to a large extent on technical supervision. Various earlier or existing rural
construction programs including PRONES by UNICEF have suffered through lack of regular field
supervision and control. Regular technical supervision of sites would be carried out by a construction
supervisor (CS) who will be based in the District. Initially the CS will supervise 5 sites but with increased
experience of builders and supervisors this maybe extended to 10 sites. During the first year of the
program the CS will be trained and supported by Senior Construction Advisors (SCA), one for each
province. These SCA's would probably need to be recruited outside the country. The CS's will supervise
and assist the community with the site preparation, collection of materials and blockmaking and supervise
the work of the building brigade or contractor. The CS will bc supervised by the coordinator or his deputy
who will certify progress payments. In Attachment 2 the cost of supervision per province has been
estimated at US$187,360 per annum or approximately 10% of the value of the works. This amount
includes the cost of SCA's which is essentially an investmcnt in training. For comparison, the fee paid by
MoH to consultants for supervising the construction of health posts in the central and northern provinces is
also about 10%.
Financial management
The procedures for controlling expenditure at community lcvcl will be similar to the "Operating Plan" of
the "Caixa Escolar" but will be adapted for school construction. The Caixa Escolar has been in existence
since 1989 and is used for the purchase of cducational materials by schools. The procedures will be
modified but would remain as close as possiblc to existing mcchanisms, but particular attention would be
given to the promptness of payments which is vitally important in a construction environment. Once
authorized, payments would be drawn from a provincial Special Account except in situations where a sub-
account at District level is possible. In Annex 5 is dcscribed the proposed financial management system.
Construction process
The PDE would announce the CSP for specific districts and distribute promotional material to
communities. Communities would apply for participation which would be appraised by a Committee,
representing PDE, the technical supervisory staff and the local authorities. All conditions for participation
and responsibilities would be agreed on. After approval, the project would be formally launched with a
ceremony and workshop. The communities would carry out agreed preparatory activities under the



Annex 13
Page 7 of 9
supervision of the CS. On completion of this task a local building brigade would be contracted to build one
classroom block with sanitary block and a well. During this first contract the brigade or contractor would
be closely supervised. On successful completion an additional classroom block would be added in the same
location or the brigade would be awarded another contract elsewhere in the district. The contracting parties
would be the brigade and the local community/school council.
There are different scenarios for the supply of materials other than local materials. The first option is to
contract a large supplier at provincial level to supply the needs of the schools in all the districts. Transport
and temporary storage in his regional depots would remain his responsibility. Some suppliers have
expressed interest in this scheme. Alternatively, the DPE develops its own capacity for transport and
storage (some provinces have this capacity) or an NGO is contracted to carry out this task. Lastly, and
probably the best option, is that the communities procure materials directly.
Organization of Community Schools Program
Diagran 1 (attached) shows the proposed organization of CSP.
Design & Management Studies
Once the project has commenced using (an) existing model(s) it will be necessary to carry out further
studies to improve the technical as well as managerial aspects of the CSP. Allowance is to be made in the
project budget for: i) the recruitment of suitable expertise in the area of facility design, engineering,
community mobilization, financial management, administration; ii) travel expenses; iii) the cost of printing
(and translating) manuals, forms and promotional materials.
IMPLEMENTATION
The implementation of CSP would begin slowly but reach an annual output of about 1,200 classrooms in
the 4th year. Output would expand as a result of the cascading effect of initial investment in training and
supervision. In the first year the program would start in three provinces covering in total about 15 districts
or 75 sites. Because construction is almost a seasonal activity in Mozambique and experience in the
community would initially be limited, the size of the projects would deliberately be kept small, involving in
most cases the addition of one 2-classroom block with ablutions and where necessary, water supply. Once
a community had successfully completed the initial task, additional classrooms and perhaps a Director's
house would be added on the same site during the next year. As set out in the table below approximately
4,300 CSP classrooms would be built in 5 years.
Annual proje ted outpu of CSP classrooms

Provinces                3         5          5        6         6

Phase 1
The first phase of CSP comprises about 1,900 classrooms, including sanitary facilities, administrative
spaces and water supply and a limited number of staff houses. This scope is defined as much by the
practical difficulties of implementing a new program as by the amount of funding likely to be available
from IDA. The duration of the first phase is 3 years. After 12 months an assessment would be made of
progress and success which would permit an early decision to expand the program during the 2nd vear or
otherwise. Training components within the program would take into account this possible expansion. The
estimated cost of the I st Phase would be about US$17 million (see Attachment 3).
Location of program
The first phase of the program would be implemented in 4 provinces based on enrollment indicators and
construction activities by other agencies. Preliminary selection would be Niassa, Cabo Delgado, Zambezia,
and Nampula.
STEPS TO BE TAKEN
Working Group and Task Force
Many of the principles and features of CSP will still need to be agreed on and further developed. This will
inter alia involve activities relating to:
I    Policy
*    State educational objectives and characteristics of effective schools.
*    State values and objectives of CSP within the framework of the Strategic Program and how these
relate to and compliment other educational activities.
*    Define community and community contribution.
*    Promote consensus and support of the program at central and provincial level.
*    Secure broad political acceptance.
2    Funding:
*    Regulatory issues pertinent to community/district or provincial control in procurement which may
affect implementation.
*    Conditions for participation by different funding agencies.
3    Capacity Building:
*    Capacity of MINED at provincial level with respect to financial and technical management,
procurement and administration of contracts.
4    Implementation
*    Finalize technical specification for schools.
*    Identify needs and priorities based on school mapping and enrollment indicators and finalize target
communities.



Annex 13
Page 9 of 9
*    Design detailed implementation plan.
*    Establish guidelines for community liaison and engagement.
*    Prepare manuals and procedures for financial management, procurement and supervision at central,
provincial and community level.
*    Organize training and training manuals.
*    State monitoring indicators.
The finalization of the Working Group on Access (and Gender) overseeing these activities and the
immediate establishment of a dedicated Sub-Group including consultants to carry out specialized parts of
the work is required. Funds for preparation activities prior to Program Effectiveness will need to be
sourced. Preparation costs are estimated at US$288,800 (see Attachment 4).
Institutional Capacity
Attachment 5 sets out the Institutional Capacity of MINED to implement the CSP sub-component. Somc
primary schools and all secondary and tertiary facilities will continue to be implemented by MINED
through the established mode of procurement.
PART 2 - SECONDARY AND TERTIARY FACILITIES
The expansion of lower secondary (ESG I) and upper secondary (ESG2) schools, teacher training institutes
(IMAP) and other capital intensive works will continue to be implemented by MINED through the
established mode of procurement. The design of some of the buildings have been modified following
concems of cost and/or of capacity. The revised schedules of accommodation and cost estimates arc shown
in Attachment 1, Table 1.3 to 1.7.
Available designs and contract documentation allow for immediate implementation of this
subcomponent.

Republic Of Mozambique
MINISTRY OF PLANNING AND FINANCE
NaIL! /GM/MPF/99
Maputo, January f- 1999
Educatioin Sector Development Program
Letter of Sector Policy
Mr. Callisto Madavo
Vice President
Africa Region
The World Bank
181 8H Street NW
Washington DC 20433
Dear Mr Madavo:
This is in reference to the current discussions about the World Bank's participation in financing part
of the Government's Strategic Plan in the context of an Education Sector Development Program.
The purpose of this letter is to set out briefly our government's rationale and objectives of the plan, to
set out the key issues in the sector and the measures the government intends to take, along with its
partners, to address these issues, and to convey governments commitments to specific sector policies,
and allocation and use of resources, that we consider essential for the successful implementation of
the plan and sustained development of the education sector.
Government Strategy
In response to the need for increased investment in human resources to support continued growth of
the economy and the expectations of Mozambicans citizens for greater educational opportunities,
the Strategic Plan aims at expanding access and equity, especially at primary and lower secondary
levels, improving the internal efficiency and improving the quality of education. The analysis of
issues in the sector also suggests that there is a need to improve the operational efficiency of the
system through restructuring of the management system, improvement of the human resources,
revision of current processes and procedures, towards increased decentralization. This would
involve decentralization of the management to the provinces, districts, municipalities, communities
and schools, that are closest to the point of use, along with transparent monitoring and
accountability arrangements to encourage efficient use of resources.



Annex 14
Page 2 of 9
The sector strategy was developed with some participation of provincial and district authorities,
intemational partners, teachers, comununities and various sectors of the civil society at large. In
developing the strategy we recognized that priority should be given to basic gencral education as the
basis for providing the mninimum educational skills to function in modem society, to add some value
for further education and training and to promote better population health and nutrition practices.
However, the government also recognizes that, while basic education is the highest priority of
education, there is an urgent need to gradually develop a strong secondary education system, to
provide candidates for much needed higher education and technical skills. We are also working on
developing appropriate complementary policies for the development of technical education and
higher education.
The program therefore aims at (a) improving the quality of education tlrough (i) upgrading teachers
by further developing the in-service distance education project and by expanding the pre-service
teacher training facilities; (ii) improving and expanding pedagogical support for teachers, and the
enhancement of teaching and learning environment; (iii) revising the curriculum with simultaneous
modifications in assessment to better monitoring student progress towards accomplishing learning
goals, and reflect regional settings and needs; (iv) increasing the provision of quality textbooks and
learning materials for primary and secondary students; and (v) improving the national assessment
and examnination system along with the curriculum revision; (b) increasing access to cducation
through (i) increasing the number of classrooms in the most undeserved areas; (ii) the development
of a strategy for maintenance of education assets; (iii) promotion of girls' education, (iv) the
establishment of a non-formal pilot education initiative; (v) encouragement of non-public provision
of education; (vi) establishment of a student welfare program, and (vii) improvement of special
education; and (c) strengthening the institutional capacity of the MOE through streamlining
functions and increasing capacity and decentralization of management of resources to the province,
district, local and school levels.
To achieve this objective the government is committed to further increase the share of public
expenditures allocated to education. In addition to the increase from less than 10 percent in 1994 to
17.3 percent in 1997 which has made education sector that commands the largest share of GOM's
recurrent expenditure, the share will be further increased to 18.2 percent in 1998. This will result
partly for the proposed salary increase for civil servants, including teachers in 1997. Specific
measures will also be introduced to further mobilize community resources anid increase efficiency in
the use of resources.
The specific sector measures we intend to take are summarized in the attached matrix, which is part
of this letter. I hope that these details will help to clarify the main directions of our education effort.
We will, of course, be willing to provide you with any further information you may require.
Yours sincerely,
Tomaz Augusto S F         ga   -
Minister of P    n      iae

MAJOR PROJECT COMPONENTS:
(i) Quality of education, including teacher training and support, curriculum revision, including environmental and health
education, learning materials supplv assessment and examinations strengthening, and direct support to schools. (ii) Access to
education, including school construction and rehabilitation, initiatives to promote girls' education. nonformal education,
special education, and support for nonpublic provision of education. (iii) Institutional capacity-building at the Ministry of
Education, including organizational restructuring and support for decentralization, policy and planning, and financial
management.
MAJOR ENVIRONMENTAL ISSUES [identified or suspected in project]:
None, ESSP will be nationwide with emphasis on rural areas. School construction will take place in rural areas and water. soil.
sand and other local materials will be needed for the construction. Sanitation facilities will be built at a number of schools
mininizing any health or environmental hazards that may arise in areas that lack of these facilities. Boreholes will be provided
for a number of existing schools without access to water and for all new schools.
OTHER ENVIRONMENTAL ISSUES [of lesser scope associated with project]: None.
PROPOSED ACTIONS [to mitigate environmental issues described aboveJ]
Proposed school construction models and designs will take into account environmental issues.
