MOZAMBIQUE
Education Sector Strategic Program (ESSP)
Teacher Training
Primary School Teachers (Gr. 1-5): The professional qualifications of primary school teachers
are highly diversified because of the variety of training models used to confront an acute shortage
of teachers. During the pre-independence period most teachers attended the EHPP teacher
training colleges composed of 4 years of primary school and 4 years of pre-service training, but
only about 3%o of the teachers remains with those qualifications. After Independence, the existing
teacher training institutes - Curso de Formacao deProfessores Primarios (CFPP) - formed the
basis for training primary school teachers, graduating about 950 teacis every year since the mid
1990s. The CFPP has taken various forms from a 1 year program to a 3 year program with a 6th
and 7th grade exam as entry requirement, respectively. Today the CFPPs are only offering the
7+3 years program. In 1997 a new teacher training program IMAP (Institutos de Magisterio
Primanio) was initiated. The program is intended to hire 10th grade graduates and provide two
years of training. Other teacher training is provided through the ADPP teacher training colleges
offering a 2 1/2 years program. In 1997 the qualifications of primary school teachers (about
29,000 teachers) were distributed as follows:
EHPP (4+4):      4%
CFPP (6+1):     39%
CFPP (7+3):     28%
ADPP (10+2 1/2): 1%
IMAP: (10+2):    1%
Untrained:      27%
Table 1 below shows the distribution of teachers by province in 1996.
Table 1:   Qualified and Untrained Teachers and Student/Teacher Ratio
by Province (1996)
Province       Total Teacher    Untrained     % Untrained    Student/Teacher

The new system of IMAPs was decided on in 1995 (Resolution 8/95) with a view of improving
the qualification of teachers. The program is designed in collaboration with the Pedagogical



Annex 16
Page 2 of 4
University. The curriculum organization for the new courses in the IMAPs cover five areas,
namely: (i) Social sciences (civic education, history and geography, (ii) Education sciences
(pedagogy, educational psychology, and school administration), (iii) Communication and
Expression (Portuguese, English, musical education, artistic/visual education, and physical
education), (iv) working activities, and (v) Mathematics and natural sciences. The weekly
training is 37 hours. Teachers graduating from the IMAPs will also teach grades 6 and 7 in the
new complete schools.
The ADPP teacher colleges (a teacher training NGO) are the only non-public teacher training
institutes recognized by the Government. The curriculum follows the IMAP program. The
ADPP program is the first to institute a 10-'-1+1.5 years teacher education program with
supervised training in the schools. Furthermore, through the training of the teachers, emphasis is
placed on the teacher's ability to develop their own materials or leaming guide (in their own
language) or use the available didactic material in innovative ways. In the program 2 1/2 years of
training is provided, with 17 months pre-service training followed by 12 months carried out in a
school in the home province of the student (language). Finally, this is followed up with a one
month training at the college. An important difference between the ADPP colleges and the public
IMAPs/CFPPs is that the ADDP colleges in general are smaller and have been better funded,
equipped and staffed with better qualified instructors. The government supports the expansion of
the ADPP teacher training colleges and the number of graduates is expected to rise from 180
graduates in 1998 to about 400 by year 2001
To increase the supply of teachers, MINED is now developing an accelerated training program
for IMAPs, so that new teachers can complete their pre-service training in 12 months and quickly
assume teaching positions in schools. For the development of this model MINED is looking to
the successful program run by the ADPP. The goal is to move to a 10+1+1 model, in which
prospective teachers who have completed ter. years of schooling will be provided with one vear
of intensive pre-service training followed by a year of supervised practice accompanied by in-
service training and support. The pre-service vear will focus on masterv of the curriculum
content and classroom "survival skills", while the in-service year will focus on the refinement of
pedagogical practice and work with parents and other community constituencies. One of the
benefits of the proposed 10+1-1 program and the experience of the ADPP program, is that it will
bring teacher trainers/instructors/tutors in contact with the schools and children through the
second year of in-school training.
In-service Teacher Training: The IMAP program is complemented by a vast expansion in the in-
service teacher training program under the Teacher Improvement Institute (IAP) and the
revitalization of the pedagogical zones (ZIPs). These programs will serve two purposes: (i)
continuing education of practicing teachers (trained or untrained) with a view of keeping teachers
abreast, maintain and upgrade their pedagogical and technical levels, and (ii) support newly
recruited teachers and upgrade the qualifications of untrained teachers. Continuing eduLcation will
be provided through the ZIPs and the LAP, using distance leaming methodologies. The in-service
program for untrained teachers consists of 50 self-learning modules (2,600 hours) and has to be
completed within four years. The students may meet in "nucleus", a specially designed location
(in a school or ZIP) where a tutor helps teachers through the learning process. The IAP program
is expected to train and upgrade 15,000 untrained teachers over five years and improve the
qualifications of another 6,000 trained teachers. The Government is including salary provisions
for teacher upgrading, and the administrative procedures for promotions are being streamlined, to
ensure a strong motivation for teachers to upgrade their skills.
ZIPs: Pedagogical zones (or ZIPs) are not a new phenomenon in Mozambique, but go back as
early as 1958 and have functioned as the focal point between 6 to 10 schools to discuss



Annex 16
Page 3 of 4
administrative and education matters. Because didactic, other learning materials and training
resources have been limited during the 1990s or sporadically disbursed, the ZIPs have mainly
functioned as a focal point for teachers to discuss problems. Under the new plan the ZIPs will be
revitalized to provide in-service training activities and other education related activities.
Teacher training courses grades 6-12: The teacher training courses from grade 6 to upper
secondary level have historically been structured as follows: For grade 6-7 through the mid-level
pedagogic institutes - Institutos Medios de Professores Primarios - (IMP) with 10th grade exam as
an entry requirement and 3 years training in bivalent courses. Under the new plan and the
introduction of complete schools (grade 1 to 7) the structure for teacher training for grades 6 and
7 will be changed, so that it would be possible to use only 2-3 teachers per class and to provide
teachers with skills to deal with multiple classes. The IMPs are currently graduating about 220
teachers per year and will be gradually phased out as the IMAP program increases the number of
graduates.
At secondary level (grades 8-12) teacher training is undertaken by the Pedagogical University
(UP) or the Eduardo Mondlane University Education Faculty with entry requirements for teacher
for lower secondary education (grades 8-9) with 9th grade exams and two years of training in two
specific disciplines. For teacher for grades 10-12 entry requirement is completion of 12th grade
and four years of training. Fewer than 100 students graduate each year from UP and UEM
together.
Supply and demand for teachers. Under the proposed expansion an estimated 18,700 teachers
will be needed from 1999-2003. The IMAPs and CFPP will graduate about 4,085 and 4,175
respectively over the sarne period. The ADPP schools are expected to graduate about 1,860
teachers.
This is assuming that the current high levels of drop-out and repetition rates, at 15% and 25%
respectively, are reduced by half. Without an improvement in the internal efficiency of the
teacher training system, there will be a need to hire about 8,400 untrained teachers as shown in
Figure 1. The Government intends to hire these teachers from the about 4,500 annual 10th grade
graduates and through an intensified and expanded in-service training program to upgrade their
pedagogical qualification. All new teachers hired as 10th grade graduates w ill receive some brief
professional orientation before they enter the classroom. Finally, the revitalization of the ZIPs
will to a large extent function as centers for teacher exchanges and assist new teachers as
described above.

MINED recognizes that the current number of pre-service teacher training graduates will be
inadequate. To cover the proposed expansion (requiring about 18,500 teachers for 1999-2003)
and improvements to the education system, the governnent will therefore rely on an expanded in-
service teacher training program. This is albout 1,500 fewer teachers than the number required if
no improvement is made to the efficiency of the system (20,000 teachers). Overall about 8,500
teachers without pre-service training will have to be hired from the 10th grade and trained
through this in-service training program. At the moment the expansion in the number of teachers
and the hiring of 10th grade graduates are viable both from a fiscal and supply side. The pressure
on the system will be further eased if the proposed improvements in the internal efficiency are
implemented for both basic education and the teacher training system. In the alternative scenario
described in Annex 4 (Table 7(b)), the population census for 1997, which estimates overall
population about 20% below the estimations based on the 1980 census, has been used. Under this
scenario the proposed expansion of the education system becomes more feasible, as the need for
teachers will go down to about 13,900 teachers for lower primary. This will ease the pressure for
hiring 1 0th grade graduates and making the upgrading of untrained teachers viable within 5-6
years.
